Source: 2026-05-29 Competing Consumers — Cloud Design Pattern | by Stellar Software Co. | May, 2026 | Dev Genius
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Competing Consumers — Cloud Design Pattern | by Stellar Software Co. | May, 2026 | Dev Genius url: https://blog.devgenius.io/competing-consumers-cloud-design-pattern-4d829c6dd1d0 date: 2026-05-29T00:10:00.669Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Dev Genius Photo by Jonathan Chng on Unsplash Ever stood in a grocery store with one checkout lane open and fifteen people in line? Now picture ten lanes opening at once. That’s Competing Consumers — except the lanes open and close based on how long the line is. What is Competing Consumers? In a distributed system, work arrives unevenly. You might get 50 requests one minute and 5,000 the next. If a single worker processes that queue sequentially, the math stops working fast. Requests pile up, latency climbs, and one slow task holds everything behind it hostage. The Competing Consumers pattern fixes this by placing a message queue between the producer and a pool of worker instances. Each worker pulls from the same queue independently. The queue ensures each message is delivered to exactly one worker — they compete for work, not duplicate it. When the queue grows, you add workers. When it shrinks, you remove them. The Competing Consumers pattern enables multiple concurrent consumers to process messages received on the same messaging channel — optimizing throughput, improving scalability and availability, and balancing the workload. The key word is compete. This is not fan-out. This is not broadcast. Each message is processed by exactly one consumer. If you have ten workers and a hundred messages, each worker handles roughly ten — not each worker handling all hundred. That’s what separates this from the Publisher-Subscriber pattern. Examples Competing Consumers show up anywhere work is parallelizable and volume is unpredictable: Azure Functions with Service Bus triggers — Functions scale out automatically based on queue depth. Each instance competes for messages from the same queue. AWS Lambda with SQS — Same idea, different cloud. Lambda spins up concurrent executions to drain the queue. Celery workers — Python’s go-to for distributed task processing. Multiple workers pull from a Redis or RabbitMQ queue. Sidekiq — Ruby’s background job processor. Worker threads compete for jobs from a shared Redis queue. Kubernetes Jobs with work queues — Pods pull tasks from a queue and terminate when the queue is empty. In our case, we used it to distribute radiological image analysis across a pool of workers — more on that shortly. Problems addressed by Competing Consumers A single processor creates a bottleneck during traffic spikes Bursty workloads make fixed capacity either wasteful or insufficient A failed processor blocks the entire pipeline — nothing behind it moves Variable processing times mean one slow task starves everything else in the queue Scaling up (bigger machine) has a ceiling; scaling out (more workers) doesn’t Our Use Case — Radiological Image Analysis We had a system that processes medical imaging studies — X-rays, CT scans, MRIs. Each image is submitted to the system, stored in a storage account, and then needs to be analyzed by an custom AI service(more on that some other time). The analysis is CPU-heavy, takes variable time (a simple X-ray is seconds; a CT with hundreds of slices is over a minute), and every image is completely independent of every other. The first version was a single Azure Function triggered by new blobs. It worked during pilot with 30 images a day. The day three more clinics onboarded, the morning queue hit 200 studies between 8 and 11 AM. A chest CT taking 90 seconds blocked everything behind it. When the function timed out on a corrupted file, it retried endlessly — while dozens of studies sat waiting. We moved to a Service Bus queue with competing workers. The imaging system enqueues a message per study — just a study ID, an image URI pointing to blob storage, and the image modality. Workers pick up messages independently. A stuck CT scan is one worker’s problem. The other nine keep processing. Architecture — the imaging system publishes to a single queue, multiple workers compete for messages Sequence — two workers compete for messages and process in parallel. Worker 2 finishes first — order doesn’t matter Implementing Competing Consumers Step 1 — Queue the work The imaging system submits a message for each study. The message is lightweight — just enough metadata to identify the work. The actual image stays in blob storage. #1 — Create a Service Bus client. In production, inject this as a singleton — don’t create one per call. #2 — Target the radiology-analysis queue. This is where all workers will compete for messages. #3 — Set MessageId to the study ID. This helps with deduplication if the sender retries. #4 — Send and move on. The imaging system doesn’t wait for analysis to finish — it just queues the work and returns. Step 2 — Worker picks up and processes The worker is an Azure Function with a Service Bus trigger. The runtime handles PeekLock automatically — when the function receives a message, the message is locked and invisible to other workers. If the function completes successfully, the message is removed. If it crashes, the lock expires and another worker picks it up. #1 — The trigger binds to the same queue. If ten function instances are running, they all compete for messages from this queue. #2 — ServiceBusMessageActions  gives you explicit control over message settlement — complete, abandon, or dead-letter. #3 — Deserialize the lightweight message payload. #4 — Download the image from the storage URI. The heavy data never flows through the queue — just the reference. #5 — Call the external analysis service. This is the slow part. Processing time varies per image. #6 — Persist the result. #7 — Complete the message. This tells Service Bus the work is done — remove the message permanently. At this point, the pattern is working. Workers compete, messages are processed in parallel, and a crashed worker doesn’t lose work. But there are two gaps: duplicate processing and poison messages. Step 3 — Idempotency and dead-letter handling If a worker takes too long and the PeekLock expires, another worker picks up the same message. Now two workers are processing the same study. You need an idempotency check. And if an image is corrupted and fails every time, you need to stop retrying and move it aside. #1 — Before doing any work, check if results already exist for this study. If another worker already finished it, skip. #2 — Complete the message without reprocessing. No wasted compute, no duplicate results. #3 — Catch unrecoverable errors. A corrupted image will fail every time — retrying won’t help. #4 — Dead-letter the message with a reason. It moves to a separate dead-letter queue where a technician can review failures without blocking the main pipeline. When to use Competing Consumers? Bursty, unpredictable workloads — Volume spikes at certain hours and drops to near-zero at others. Fixed capacity is either wasteful or insufficient. Independent tasks — Each message can be processed in isolation. No dependency on other messages or processing order. Variable processing times — Some tasks are fast, some are slow. A pool of workers prevents slow tasks from blocking fast ones. High availability requirements — If one worker fails, others keep processing. No single point of failure. Cost-sensitive scaling — You want to scale to zero during idle periods and scale out during peaks, paying only for what you use. You don’t need this when tasks must run in a strict sequence, when each task depends on the result of the previous one, or when your workload is steady and low enough that a single processor handles it comfortably. Adding a queue and managing worker pools introduces complexity — don’t add it if a simple loop solves the problem. Competing Consumers vs. Other Patterns This pattern is commonly confused with two others: Competing Consumers vs. Publisher-Subscriber — In Publisher-Subscriber, every subscriber gets a copy of every message — it’s fan-out. In Competing Consumers, each message goes to exactly one worker — it’s load distribution. They solve different problems. You can combine them: a Service Bus Topic fans out to multiple subscriptions, and each subscription has its own pool of competing workers. Competing Consumers vs. Queue-Based Load Leveling — Load Leveling uses a queue as a buffer to smooth out traffic spikes, but it doesn’t prescribe multiple consumers. You could have a single consumer behind that queue. Competing Consumers takes it further — multiple workers draining the queue in parallel. Load Leveling is the buffer; Competing Consumers is the workforce behind it. Issues with Competing Consumers Message ordering is not guaranteed. Workers process at different speeds. A message enqueued first might finish last. If your workflow requires strict ordering, this pattern fights you. Service Bus supports sessions to group related messages to the same consumer, but that constrains parallelism — which defeats the purpose. Duplicate processing is possible. At-least-once delivery means a message can be processed more than once — especially when PeekLock expires before a slow worker finishes. Your workers must be idempotent. Check before you write. Upsert instead of insert. Design for “this might run twice” from the start. Poison messages can stall a worker. A malformed message that crashes the worker will reappear on the queue after lock expiry, get picked up again, crash again — a retry loop. Set a maximum delivery count and route failures to a dead-letter queue. Service Bus does this automatically when MaxDeliveryCount is exceeded. Scaling is not free. More workers means more connections to downstream services, more concurrent database writes, more compute cost. Your downstream dependencies need to handle the fan-in. If ten workers all call the same analysis API simultaneously, that API becomes the new bottleneck. Monitoring complexity increases. With a single processor, you have one log stream. With N workers, you have N. Correlate everything with a studyId or correlation ID — otherwise debugging a failed message across workers is guesswork. Summary Competing Consumers places a queue between producers and a pool of workers — each message is processed by exactly one worker Workers scale out based on queue depth and scale down (or to zero) when idle PeekLock ensures a crashed worker doesn’t lose a message — it reappears for another worker to pick up Idempotency is your responsibility — at-least-once delivery means duplicates are possible Dead-letter queues catch poison messages that fail repeatedly, keeping the main pipeline healthy This pattern solves bursty, parallelizable, independent workloads — not ordered or dependent ones Conclusion The Competing Consumers pattern is one of those ideas that sounds obvious in hindsight — put a queue in front, add more workers. But the value is in what it gives you without custom code: automatic load distribution, fault tolerance through PeekLock, cost-efficient scaling, and a clean separation between producing work and doing work. In our case, it meant the imaging system never had to care about how many studies were in the pipeline. It enqueued a message and moved on. Whether one worker or twenty picked it up was the infrastructure’s problem. When the morning rush hit, workers scaled out. When the queue drained, they scaled down. A corrupted image didn’t block the pipeline — it landed in the dead-letter queue for a human to review. The real insight is this: Competing Consumers isn’t about speed. It’s about resilience. The throughput gain is a side effect. The real value is that no single failure — a crashed worker, a slow task, a corrupted input — can take down the pipeline. Every message gets its fair shot, and the system keeps moving. Written & edited by — Vivek Mittal https://www.linkedin.com/in/vivekmittal06/ We write about Computer Programs & Technology.

Source: 2026-05-29 Zero Copy in Kafka. Kafka doesn’t move data. It tells the… | by Satnam Singh | Mar, 2026 | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Zero Copy in Kafka. Kafka doesn’t move data. It tells the… | by Satnam Singh | Mar, 2026 | Medium url: https://medium.com/@satnamgoldi/zero-copy-in-kafka-ee2f2ee199ee date: 2026-05-29T00:09:42.781Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Let’s start with the problem Imagine you have to send a file from disk to a network socket. In a traditional system, here is what happens step by step. The OS reads data from disk into the kernel buffer. Then it copies that data from the kernel buffer into a user-space buffer — the memory your application controls. Then your application hands it back to the OS, which copies it into a socket buffer. Then, finally, the data goes out over the network. That’s four context switches and two unnecessary data copies — from kernel to user space, then back again. Your application never actually needed to look at the data. It just passed it through. This is the normal way. And at low volumes, it’s fine. But at Kafka’s scale — millions of messages per second — those extra copies become the bottleneck. And this is exactly the kind of thing you don’t notice… until traffic scales. What zero copy actually means The name is a bit misleading. It doesn’t mean zero copies at all. It just means: no copying through user space. Instead of pulling data into the application and then pushing it back, the OS handles the transfer internally. The application basically says, “send this file” … and steps out of the way. In Linux, this happens using a system call called — sendfile() With sendfile(),the flow becomes: disk → kernel buffer → socket buffer → network No unnecessary detour through your application. Less copying. Less CPU. Less overhead. Now the interesting part — page cache This is where Kafka gets really clever. Operating systems already have a built-in mechanism called the page cache. Whenever data is read from disk, the OS keeps a copy in RAM. So the next read doesn’t go to disk — it comes straight from memory. Pretty standard OS behavior. But Kafka leans into this completely. Kafka’s design choice (which feels weird at first) Kafka writes everything to disk. At first, that sounds like a bad idea. Why write to disk if you care about speed? But here’s the trick. Those writes are sequential, which disks are actually very good at. And more importantly, the OS caches that data in memory anyway. So what really happens is: Kafka writes to disk OS keeps it in the page cache Consumers read from memory So even though it looks like disk I/O… most of the time it isn’t. Kafka is basically using the OS as its cache. And honestly, the OS is much better at this than most application-level caching. Why not just use JVM memory? This is an important one. Most Java systems try to cache data in heap memory. But that comes with a cost — garbage collection. As the heap grows, GC pauses kick in. At high throughput, even a 200ms pause is painful. Kafka avoids that entire problem. No heavy heap caching. No GC pressure for message storage. It lets the OS manage memory instead. Also — small but important point — page cache survives process restarts. Your JVM dies and comes back… the cache is still warm. Where zero copy and page cache meet Now combine both ideas. When a consumer fetches messages, Kafka’s broker calls sendfile(). If the data is still in page cache (which it usually is), the flow becomes: page cache → socket → network No disk read. No JVM involvement. No extra copies. The broker is barely doing anything at this point. It’s mostly the OS moving data around. One catch (and it matters in production) This works great… until you enable SSL. Once encryption is enabled, Kafka must read the data into the user space to encrypt it, which breaks the zero-copy path. So now: More CPU usage More memory movement Slight drop in throughput Most systems still enable SSL (obviously), but it’s good to know what you’re trading off. Why this matters in real systems This is a big reason why Kafka can handle insane throughput without crazy hardware. Give it decent RAM, and the OS will keep most hot data in memory. Consumers read from RAM. sendfile() pushes it to the network efficiently. Disk becomes less of a bottleneck than you’d expect. And CPU stays relatively calm because it’s not busy copying data around. Final thoughts Zero copy and the OS page cache are not Kafka-specific inventions. They are OS-level capabilities that have existed for decades. What Kafka does differently is design its entire architecture around them — sequential writes, no JVM heap caching, sendfile() for consumer delivery. Understanding this helps you make better operational decisions: why giving Kafka brokers more RAM matters more than faster CPUs, why sequential disk I/O is a feature, not a compromise, and why encryption has a measurable throughput cost on Kafka specifically.

Source: 2026-05-29 Kafka Rebalancing Explained (Part 1): Eager vs Cooperative | by Lenon Rodrigues | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Kafka Rebalancing Explained (Part 1): Eager vs Cooperative | by Lenon Rodrigues | Medium url: https://medium.com/@lenonrodrigues/kafka-rebalancing-explained-part-1-eager-vs-cooperative-d9dfc6fd5b8f date: 2026-05-29T00:09:29.499Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap This article is Part 1 of a short series on Kafka rebalancing in production. In Part 2, we’ll dive into partition assignment strategies (Range, RoundRobin, Sticky, and CooperativeSticky). Then, Part 3 will help you choose the right approach for your workload (event-driven microservices vs ETL/log pipelines). Finally Part 4 will cover production hardening: tuning, reducing rebalance storms, and troubleshooting consumer groups. Introduction At its core, Kafka is built around topics, partitions, and consumer groups, which are the fundamental components that allow Kafka to manage high volumes of data across many consumers efficiently. One of the key features of Kafka is its ability to dynamically balance partitions among consumers within a consumer group, which is handled by a process known as partition assignment or rebalancing. Kafka Core Concepts: Topics, Partitions, and Consumers Before we explore the assignment strategies, it’s important to understand the key components of Kafka that are affected by these strategies: Topics: Kafka topics are logical channels to which producers send data, and from which consumers read. Topics are the high-level abstraction in Kafka that organize the flow of messages. Partitions: Each topic is divided into one or more partitions. Partitions are the unit of parallelism in Kafka, they allow data in a single topic to be distributed across multiple brokers. Partitions are also ordered, meaning messages within a partition retain their sequence, but messages between partitions are not ordered. Consumer Groups: Kafka consumers are grouped into consumer groups. Each consumer in a group reads data from one or more partitions of a topic, and Kafka ensures that no two consumers in the same group read from the same partition at the same time. Consumers within a group collaboratively consume all messages from the topic partitions. Group Coordinator: is the broker (specifically, the leader of the __consumer_offsets partition mapped to your group.id) that manages a consumer group: it tracks membership and heartbeats, detects failures, orchestrates rebalances via JoinGroup/SyncGroup, persists offsets, and distributes the partition assignment decided by the group leader. What is Kafka Rebalancing? Kafka rebalancing is the process of redistributing partitions among the consumers in a consumer group. This occurs when there are changes in the group membership, such as: A new consumer joins the group A consumer leaves the group New partitions are added to a topic Partitions are removed from a topic Rebalancing ensures that the load is evenly distributed across consumers and that no consumer is overloaded with too many partitions while others remain idle. However, frequent rebalancing can lead to interruptions in processing, so it’s important to choose an appropriate strategy to minimize its impact. There are two types of rebalances, depending on the partition assignment strategy that the consumer group uses: Eager Rebalance In an eager rebalance, every consumer in the group briefly “drops everything.” As soon as a membership or topology change is detected, such as a new consumer joining, an existing one leaving, or partitions being added all members stop fetching, revoke ownership of all partitions, rejoin the group, and receive a brand-new assignment. This creates a short window of unavailability for the entire group: no member is reading while everyone is simultaneously giving up their partitions and waiting for the coordinator to distribute a fresh map. The figure below illustrates the two distinct phases of an eager rebalance. First, all members relinquish their partitions; only after everyone has completed this does the group receive the new assignment and resume consumption. Operationally, the eager protocol is straightforward and predictable, there are fewer intermediate states to reason about, and once the new assignment lands, everyone resumes from a clean slate. The trade-off is the stop-the-world effect. Cooperative rebalances In a cooperative (incremental) rebalance, the group avoids the stop-the-world pause by moving only the partitions that actually need to change hands. Consumers keep processing every partition they are allowed to retain, and relinquish ownership only of the subset flagged for reassignment. The process unfolds in multiple phases. First, after a membership or topology change, the group leader proposes a partial redistribution and notifies each consumer which partitions it will lose. Those consumers immediately stop fetching from just those partitions and revoke their ownership, while continuing to read from all others. In the next phase, once the coordinator confirms that the partitions are free, the leader assigns them to their new owners, who begin consuming without disturbing the rest of the group. Because the redistribution is incremental, the protocol may require a few short iterations before the assignment is fully stable, especially when many members or topics are involved. Even so, the effect on availability is dramatically better than with the eager approach: most consumers never pause, most partitions never move, and caches and prefetch buffers stay warm. This difference becomes decisive in large consumer groups and in stateful or latency-sensitive workloads, where a full reset would translate into long lag spikes and cold starts. When to use Cooperative vs Eager (a story from production) Imagine a typical event-driven flow in microservices: an Order Service emits OrderCreated. Within seconds, three consumers must react: Inventory Service holds stock Payment Service authorizes a charge Notification Service sends confirmation Everything is fine until you deploy a new version of the Inventory consumer. If your group uses Eager rebalance The moment the new pod joins, Kafka triggers a rebalance and the group briefly goes silent: all consumers stop fetching all partitions are revoked the group reforms and gets a brand-new assignment For a few seconds (sometimes longer), no one is consuming. Messages pile up, lag spikes, and the flow becomes visible: Payment authorizations arrive late and hit timeouts Inventory holds lag behind, causing “out of stock” false negatives Retries kick in, producing extra load and duplicate processing risk It’s not that Kafka is broken — it’s doing exactly what eager means: stop-the-world. If your group uses Cooperative rebalance Now replay the same deploy with cooperative mode. Kafka still rebalances, but it does it incrementally: most consumers keep consuming the partitions they can retain only the partitions that must move are revoked the “new” consumer starts taking ownership gradually So instead of the whole flow pausing, only a slice of partitions changes hands, while the rest of the event pipeline stays hot. Lag might bump slightly, but you avoid the “everything froze” moment that breaks SLAs. The takeaway If your system is event-driven and users feel delays, pick Cooperative. If your pipeline is batch-ish / ETL / logs and pauses are fine, Eager is OK. In production, the choice often comes down to whether your workload can tolerate stop-the-world behavior during scaling/restarts and how costly it is to “warm up” consumption again. In short, eager favors simplicity, while cooperative favors availability during change. If your workloads are small, stable, and can tolerate brief pauses, eager may be enough. But if you run large, stateful, or latency-sensitive event-driven systems, cooperative is usually the better fit. In the next article, we’ll look at assignment strategies and how Kafka decides which consumer gets which partitions after a rebalance. I am a Brazilian software engineer who loves to write about development.

Source: 2026-05-29 Understanding Kafka Storage and Replication: A Deep Dive | by Divesh Kumar chordia | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Understanding Kafka Storage and Replication: A Deep Dive | by Divesh Kumar chordia | Medium url: https://medium.com/@diveshkumarchordia/understanding-kafka-storage-and-replication-a-deep-dive-into-event-streaming-architecture-6b0547210fd5 date: 2026-05-29T00:09:16.176Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Every few days I get back to relearning, and some topics that seemed to brush off suddenly come back with better clarity and more information when rereading. I was reading “Kafka: The Definitive Guide” and as I just started, this was today’s learning that I wanted to share. Kafka is a powerful distributed event streaming platform. But what actually happens after you produce a message to Kafka? How and where does Kafka store your data, and how does it ensure it stays safe and available? Let’s walk through Kafka internals step-by-step. What is a Kafka Broker and Disk Storage? In Kafka, a broker is a server responsible for receiving messages from producers. When a message is received, the broker assigns it an offset — a unique sequential ID — and then commits the message to storage on disk. Here, “disk” refers to the broker’s local file system storage. Specifically, Kafka stores messages in log files, which are persistent files stored on the disk. This approach ensures the messages are durable, surviving broker restarts or failures. The Role of Topics, Partitions, and Segments Topic and Partition A topic is essentially a named stream of related events or messages. For scalability and parallelism, each topic is divided into one or more partitions. Partitions are the fundamental units of storage and ordering in Kafka. Each partition is an append-only, ordered sequence of messages. This sequence lives on disk on a broker as a directory containing log segment files. Segment Files Partitions are not a single file but composed of multiple log segment files. Each segment file stores a continuous chunk of messages starting at a specific offset. Kafka writes messages sequentially to the active segment file until it reaches a configured size or time limit. Then it closes that segment and creates a new one to continue writing. This design allows Kafka to efficiently handle large volumes of data and perform fast sequential disk writes. How is a Message Stored? Each message event (e.g., a user purchase event in JSON): … is serialized into bytes and appended atomically to one log segment file in the partition. Multiple messages get appended sequentially inside the segment file. However: A single event/message does not span multiple segment files. Many messages batch up inside one segment, but each message is fully contained in exactly one segment file. Example of Storage Layout For a topic orders with partition orders-0, the broker's disk might contain files like: Each .log file holds raw serialized messages, and .index files help Kafka quickly locate messages by offset. What is a Partition, Really? Partitions help Kafka scale horizontally and maintain order: Each partition is an independent ordered log. Clients read and write messages in order within a specific partition. Kafka balances partitions across brokers so many consumers can process data in parallel. Replication: Making Kafka Fault Tolerant Kafka ensures high availability and durability by replicating partitions across multiple brokers. Each partition has an associated replication factor (commonly 3). This means for partition orders-0, for example, there are 3 copies on 3 different brokers. Only one broker is the leader for a partition, handling all reads/writes. Other brokers are followers replicating the leader’s log asynchronously. If a leader broker dies, a follower is elected leader automatically to avoid downtime. All replicas have the same messages, offsets, and segments. Broker Role Partition Copy Broker 1 Leader orders-0 Broker 2 Follower orders-0 replica Broker 3 Follower orders-0 replica Replication guarantees that data won’t be lost if a broker fails, providing durability and data availability. Summary Events/messages get serialized and fully appended to a single segment file within a partition. Partitions split topics into ordered, immutable logs to enable scalable parallel processing. Segment files store batches of messages inside partitions on disk, rotated by size/time. Replication ensures each partition’s data is copied across multiple brokers for fault tolerance. Kafka assigns unique offsets to messages for precise location and ordering. This architecture allows Kafka to be a highly durable, scalable, and fault-tolerant system suitable for massive event streaming workloads. This was my today’s learning session summary. Writing this down helped me understand these concepts better, and hopefully it’s useful for others who are also learning Kafka fundamentals.

Source: 2026-05-29 The Architecture of GCP Pub/Sub: Control Planes, Data Planes, and Global Scalability | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: The Architecture of GCP Pub/Sub: Control Planes, Data Planes, and Global Scalability | Medium url: https://medium.com/@d.sathwik99/inside-gcp-pub-sub-storage-delivery-reliability-and-scaling-7d13ddee31d7 date: 2026-05-29T00:09:04.108Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Why Pub/Sub came into existence? Before defining google cloud Pub/Sub, it is better to understand the architecture problem it solves. As software systems grow, direct service-to-service communication becomes difficult to manage. In many designs, one service calls another service synchronously (for example through API/RPC call), which means caller waits for a response before continuing. This works at small scale, but it creates problems as the number of services increases. Pub/Sub exists to reduce these problems by enabling asynchronous communication and decoupling producers from consumers. Google documentation describes Pub/Sub as an asynchronous, scalable service that decouples services producing messages from services processing them. Let’s use a simple e-commerce example. When a customer places an order, the checkout service may need to inform multiple other services: Inventory service (reduce stock). Shipping Service (Create Shipment). Email Service (Send Receipt). At first this looks fine, checkout can directly call each service. But as the system grows,this design creates problems. Too many direct connections (Sphaghetti Problem): Checkout is no longer connected to just 3 services. Over time, it may also need to call: Fraud service Analytics service Notification service. Loyalty points service. Audit logging service. Now check out has many direct integrations. The more services you add, the more connection and dependencies you create. This makes the systems harder to understand, test and maintain. Waiting on other services (Blocking problem) Suppose checkout directly call inventory, Shipping and Email during order processing. Inventory responds quickly (Works fine). Shipping responds (works fine). Email service is broken or slow or down (Interrupts). If checkout is waiting for email to respond, the customer may see a delay or even a failure message. So even though the payment was successful, one non critical service can affect the user experience. This is called a blocking depenency: one service gets stuck waiting for another. Small changes become big changes (Change Amplification) Now imagine the business adds a fraud check service. With direct integrations, engineers may need to update the checkout service code to Call the new service. handle timeouts or retries. Handle errors. Decide whether checkout should fail if fraud service is unavailable. A single new requirement causes changes in existing services and logic. That is change amplification: One change spreads into many places. The core idea: Asynchronous decoupling A common solution is to introduce a message broker between producers and consumers. Instead of service A directly calling services B, C and D and waiting for each of them, Service A publishes an event once. Consumers receive and process that event independently. This changes the system behaviour in an important way: The producer and consumer do not need to run at the same time. The producer does not need to know who all consumers are. Slow or temporarily unavailable consumers do not necessarily block the producer. This is the architectural role Pub/Sub plays in many cloud systems. Google’docs explicitly describe Pub/Sub as asynchronous messaging and contrast it with synchronous RPC style interaction, noting the flexibility and robustness benefits of asynchronous integration. Why Pub/Sub is also described as Reliable and Scalable Asynchronous decoupling explains why Pub/Sub exists. The next question is: Why is google cloud Pub/Sub described as highly reliable and scalable? Google’s Pub/Sub architecture documentation frames the performance of a messaging system using three dimensions: Scalability, availability and latency. It also notes that these dimensions are often in tension, so the system must balance them carefully rather than maximizing all three at once. Reliability Reliability means Pub/Sub is built so your messages are not easily lost, even when something goes wrong. When your application sends a message, Pub/Sub does not immediately say “Sucess” just because it received the request. It first stores the message safely, and only then sends an acknowledgment back to the publisher. Pub/Sub is also designed to handle failure such as: A server crasying. A software issue. Or sudden traffic spikes. Google uses replication (copying message data across multiple zones) so that if one zone has a problem, the message is still available from another zone. The main benefit is this: your application does not need to build all this reliability logic itself, Pub/Sub handles much of that complexity for us. Scalability: Scalability means Pub/Sub can continue working well even when your system grows a lot. That growth is not just “more messages per second”. A Pub/Sub system can become heavier in many different ways such as More topics. More publishers and subscribers. Larger message sizes. Faster message traffic. Larger backlogs (messages waiting to be processed). So a scalable system must handle growth in multiple directions, not just once. Pub/Sub is built to scale horizontally, which means google adds and adjusts resources behind the scenes as traffic changes. Because Pub/Sub is a managed service developers usually do not need to manage broker machines or manually plan partitions the way they often would in self managed systems. That is why Pub/Sub is often easier to operate, you focus on your application logic, while google handles much of scaling infrastructure. Latency Latency in Pub/Sub should be discussed using two separate measurements, because they represent different parts of the system. Publish acknowledgment latency: The time from when a publisher sends a message to when Pub/Sub acknowledges the publish. Delivery Latency: The time from when the message is published to when it reaches a subscriber. These metrics should be analyzed separately because they can have different bottlenecks. Publish acknowledgment latency is influenced by publish path inside Pub/Sub. Delivery latency is influenced by both the Pub/Sub delivery path and subscriber side conditions such as subscriber capacity, flow control, processing speed, backlog size. In distributed systems, you do not get unlimited scale, perfect availability, zero latency simultaneously. These factors are often at odds and require trade offs. This are related to distributed systems thinking including CAP theorem, but not idential to those theorems. The practical takeaway is simple: Improving durability or availability often requires extra coordination or replication. Extra coordination usually adds more latency. Scaling under load can also change latency behavior. Pub/Sub Architecture (Simple Mental Model) Let’s understand this with an example: Imagine a global e commerce company with one topic called: order-events Checkout service in Tokyo publishes an order event. Checkout service in Sydney publishes another order event. Checkout service in New York publishes another event. Each publisher connects to a nearby pub/sub endpoint for low latency. Pub/Sub then stores each message in an allowed region based on polict or routing rules. Over time, the same topic may contain messages stored in multiple regions. Now a fraud detection pipeline running in london subscribes to order-event. The fraud service does not need to manually connect to servers in japan, australia, US. It connects to a nearby Pub/Sub server, and pub/sub handles the internal aggregation and delivery. That is what makes the system feel like one unified global event bus to the application team. The key points are: Publishers and subscribers do not need to know which specific server or region they are talking to. Pub/Sub handles routing automatically. Google routes traffic efficiently while respecting your storage policy rules. A single message is stored in one region. But a topic can contain messages stored in many regions. Pub/Sub is divided into major parts: Control plane -> Assigns publishers and subscribers to message-serving servers Data plane -> Actually moves messages publishers and subscribers. Control Plane: The control plane is the part of the Pub/Sub that decides which Pub/Sub servers your application should connect to. It does not carry your messages. Its job is to make smart connection assignments so the system stays fast, stable and balanced. Google calls the control plane servers as routers and the message handling server as forwarders. When a client connects to Pub/Sub, a router decides which data centers the client should use, based on network distance, latency, and other factors. Within a selected data center, the router then distributed clients across available forwarders to balance load. Router tries to balance two goals at the same time: Uniform load: Spread client evenly so no forwarder is overloaded. Stable assignments: Avoid moving too many clients when load changes or server come or go. To manage this trade off, the router uses a varient of consistent hashing and returns ordered list of forwarders the client can try. That list may change depending on forwarder availability and the client’s traffic pattern. Google also mentions a service proxy between clients and forwarders. Its job is to handle connection optimization so application clients do not need to manage these routing details themselves. In plain english terms: Our app does not need to manually choose the best Pub/Sub server. Pub/Sub handles that behind the scenes. Data plane: Once a publisher or subscriber is connected to Pub/Sub forwarder, the data plane takes over. The data plane is the part of Pub/Sub that actually receives, stores and delivers messages. The easiest way to understand it is to follow, the life of one message. Assume the topic has at least once subscription attached. A publisher application sends a message to a Pub/Sub topic. The message is routed (through the proxy layer) to a publishing forwarder. A data plane server handling that publisher’s connection. Pub/Sub writes the message to storage immediately so it can be delivered reliably. This storage first behavior is why Pub/Sub can safely acknowledge the publisher after persistence, instead of acknowledging too early. After the message is persisted, Pub/Sub sends an acknowledgment back to the publisher and guarantees delivery to all subscriptions attached to that topic. At the same time as the storage or persistance work. Pub/Sub starts making the message available to subscribers. Subscribers receive messages through subscribing forwarders. Once a subscriber process the message, it sends an acknowledgment back to Pub/Sub. That acknowledgment flows through the subscribing forwarder back to the publishing side for meta data or state tracking. When the message has been acknowledged for all subscriptions on that topic, Pub/Sub deletes it from storage asynchronously.

Source: 2026-05-29 Unleashing the Power of Event-Driven Messaging Architecture: A Deep Dive into Pub-Sub Model | by Ansh Narula | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Unleashing the Power of Event-Driven Messaging Architecture: A Deep Dive into Pub-Sub Model | by Ansh Narula | Medium url: https://medium.com/@anshnarula55/unleashing-the-power-of-event-driven-architecture-a-deep-dive-into-pub-sub-model-and-aws-services-c09f5f42a8ca date: 2026-05-29T00:08:51.061Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap In today’s fast-paced digital landscape, businesses strive to deliver real-time responsiveness and scalability in their systems. This is where event-driven messaging architecture comes into play. By leveraging event-driven principles, businesses can achieve seamless communication, decoupling of components, and efficient processing of events. In this article, we will delve into the concept of event-driven messaging architecture, provide real-world examples, explore its benefits for businesses, and demonstrate how to implement it using AWS SNS, SQS, Lambda, and DLQ. Additionally, we will explore the pub-sub model, a key component of event-driven architectures. Pub-Sub Model The publish-subscribe (pub-sub) model is a messaging pattern that enables the decoupling of components in an event-driven architecture. In this model, publishers are responsible for producing events or messages, while subscribers register their interest in specific topics or event types. When a publisher sends an event to a topic, the event is distributed to all subscribed subscribers, allowing for asynchronous and scalable communication. Pub-Sub Model Case Study: LinkedIn Notifications In platforms like LinkedIn, the pub-sub model is commonly used to enable users to receive real-time notifications based on their interests. Let’s try to understand each steps: Publisher — LinkedIn Platform LinkedIn serves as the publisher, responsible for generating events when specific actions occur, such as profile views, connection requests, job opportunities, or updates from connections. Subscribers — LinkedIn Users Users on LinkedIn are the subscribers who express their interest in receiving notifications related to activities happening within their professional network. Topics — User Activities These can be user activities like: connection requests, profile views, job opportunities, skill endorsements, etc. Workflow When a relevant activity occurs, such as someone sending a connection request, LinkedIn generates an event associated with that activity. The event is published to the corresponding topic related to the specific type of activity. Users who have subscribed to those topics receive notifications about the events through various channels, including the LinkedIn website, mobile app push notifications, or email notifications. The platform delivers the notifications to the subscribers in real-time. Subscribers can interact with the notifications, such as accepting connection requests, exploring job opportunities, or engaging with updates from connections, directly from the notification. This pattern ensures a seamless and engaging user experience, allowing users to interact with content as soon as it is published by their subscribed accounts. Understanding Event-Driven Messaging Architecture using AWS Event-driven messaging architecture revolves around the exchange of events among different components of a system. Events represent significant occurrences or changes in the system, such as user interactions, system events, or data updates. Rather than relying on tightly coupled systems, event-driven architectures enable loose coupling by ensuring that components are notified of events and can react accordingly. Although there are many other technologies available for building event-driven messaging infrastructure, we have chosen to leverage several AWS services for this task. AWS provides a comprehensive suite of services that offer numerous benefits for building scalable and robust event-driven architectures. Let’s explore the ke,y components involved: SNS (Simple Notification Service): A fully managed pub-sub messaging service. Provides message publishing and delivery to subscribers. Supports multiple communication protocols and delivery mechanisms, such as HTTP, email, SMS, and mobile push notifications. Allows topic-based event routing and fanout, where a single event can be sent to multiple subscribers. 2. SQS (Simple Queue Service): A fully managed message queuing service. Enables decoupling of components and asynchronous communication between services. Provides reliable message storage and delivery. Offers two types of queues: standard queues for high-throughput messaging and FIFO (First-In-First-Out) queues for ordered messaging. 3. AWS Lambda: A serverless compute service. Allows running code without provisioning or managing servers. Supports event-driven architecture by enabling functions to respond to events from various sources. Automatically scales based on the incoming request volume. Supports a wide range of programming languages. 4. DLQ (Dead Letter Queue): A mechanism to handle and store failed or unprocessed messages. Captures messages that couldn’t be successfully processed by the consumer. Provides an opportunity for error analysis and debugging. Allows for implementing retry mechanisms or manual intervention for problematic messages. Event flow: An event occurs in a service (such as a change in a db table). The service publishes a message to an SNS topic. The SNS topic fans-out the message to the subscribed SQS queues. The SQS queues store the message until an consumer retrieves it for processing. The consumer then polls out the message from queue, if the message is successfully processed, it deletes it from the queue. If the consumer fails to process the message, pollers deletes the message from queue and the message is sent to DLQ. The DLQ stores the messages until a consumer retrieves it for analysis or reprocessing. Fanout architecture Fanout is a technique often used in pub-sub architectures to achieve message distribution to multiple subscribers efficiently. In a fanout architecture, a message is delivered to multiple recipients without the publisher being aware of the subscribers or their specific identities. It involves replicating the message and sending it to each subscriber independently. Customer order processing Let’s take an example of order processing done by an e-commerce business. We will understand how businesses can handle a large number of customer orders and process them efficiently. Customer places an order through the e-commerce website or application. Order details are sent as an event to SNS. SNS publishes the “Order Placed Event” to a topic. The topic is configured to send the event to SQS queues. Each SQS queue represents a different component or service for processing specific aspects of the order. Lambda functions are triggered by their respective SQS queues. Multiple instances of the Lambda function can run in parallel. Lambda function retrieves order details and performs business logic.
One Lambda function may handle inventory management and update the stock levels.
Another Lambda function may handle datalake ingestion.
Yet another Lambda function may handle shipping logistics and generate shipping labels. Lambda function generates a response, such as order confirmation or error message. Successful order processing removes the event from the SQS queue. Parallel Processing and Scalability: With the fanout architecture, each Lambda function subscribed to an SQS queue independently processes the order event. The fanout pattern allows for parallel processing of the order across multiple services, enhancing scalability and performance. As the load increases, AWS automatically scales the number of Lambda instances to handle the incoming order events efficiently. Conclusion In conclusion, event-driven messaging architecture, coupled with the power of AWS services such as SNS, SQS, and Lambda, empowers businesses to achieve seamless communication, scalability, and efficient processing of events. The pub-sub model allows for decoupling of components and asynchronous communication, ensuring that components are notified of events and can react accordingly. By leveraging AWS services, businesses can benefit from the following advantages: Fully managed services: AWS provides fully managed services like SNS, SQS, and Lambda, which reduce the operational burden of setting up and managing infrastructure. Scalability: AWS services automatically scale based on the incoming request volume, allowing businesses to handle a large number of events efficiently. Reliability: SQS provides reliable message storage and delivery, ensuring that messages are not lost in transit. Flexibility: Lambda supports a wide range of programming languages, giving businesses the freedom to write functions in their preferred language. Error handling: DLQ (Dead Letter Queue) provides a mechanism to capture and store failed or unprocessed messages, enabling error analysis, debugging, and implementing retry mechanisms. References: https://aws.amazon.com/what-is/pub-sub-messaging/ https://medium.com/geekculture/the-event-notification-pattern-a62d48519107 https://aws.amazon.com/event-driven-architecture/ https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/compute/messaging-fanout-pattern-for-serverless-architectures-using-amazon-sns/ Thanks for reading and kudos to you for reaching the conclusion. Happy coding  🚀 Tech enthusiast. Actively looking for SDE-1 jobs.

Source: 2026-05-29 The Secret Life of AWS: The Fanout (Amazon SNS & Pub/Sub) | by Aaron Rose | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: The Secret Life of AWS: The Fanout (Amazon SNS & Pub/Sub) | by Aaron Rose | Medium url: https://medium.com/@aaron.rose.tx/the-secret-life-of-aws-the-fanout-amazon-sns-pub-sub-5886f7f2cba3 date: 2026-05-29T00:08:39.121Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap How to broadcast one event to multiple microservices. Part 47 of The Secret Life of AWS Timothy’s Checkout service was finally stable. By routing messages through Amazon SQS, the Checkout process was completely insulated from Inventory database outages. But success brings new requirements. “The product team wants to expand,” Timothy told Margaret, mapping out the new flow on the whiteboard. “When a customer clicks ‘Buy’, we still need to update Inventory. But now, we also need to notify the Fulfillment service to print a shipping label, and we need to trigger the Notification service to email a receipt.” Timothy drew three arrows pointing out of the Checkout service. “I am going to update the Checkout code. Instead of sending one message to the inventory-queue, it will loop and send three separate messages: one to inventory-queue, one to fulfillment-queue, and one to email-queue." Margaret shook her head and handed him an eraser. “You are re-coupling the architecture, Timothy.” “How?” Timothy asked. “They are all asynchronous queues. Checkout isn’t waiting for any of them to finish.” “But Checkout now has to know about all of them,” Margaret explained. “If we add a Data Analytics queue next month, you have to rewrite and redeploy the Checkout code to send a fourth message. The Checkout service shouldn’t care who is listening. It should only care that an order was placed.” “We need to implement the Publish/Subscribe (Pub/Sub) pattern using Amazon SNS.” The Broadcaster (Amazon SNS) Margaret opened the AWS Console and navigated to Amazon Simple Notification Service (SNS). “SQS is for one-to-one messaging,” she explained. “A message goes into a queue, and one consumer pulls it out. SNS is for one-to-many broadcasting. We use it to announce events to the entire architecture.” She created a new SNS Topic and named it order-placed-topic. “This is our logical access point,” Margaret said. “From now on, the Checkout service does exactly one thing: it publishes a single JSON event to this SNS Topic. That is it. Its job is done.” The Fanout Pattern “But if Checkout is publishing to a Topic, how do the messages get into the SQS queues so the other services can poll them?” Timothy asked. “Through Subscriptions,” Margaret replied. She navigated to the new SNS Topic and clicked Create subscription. She selected Amazon SQS as the protocol and pointed it to the inventory-queue. She repeated the process for the fulfillment-queue and the email-queue. “This is the SNS to SQS Fanout pattern,” she explained. “When Checkout publishes one message to the SNS Topic, SNS instantly pushes a copy of that exact message into all three SQS queues simultaneously. SNS topics can even deliver to SQS queues in completely different AWS accounts, making this perfect for large organizations with strict account boundaries.” “The beauty of this is absolute architectural decoupling,” she noted. “The publisher (Checkout) has no idea who the subscribers are. The subscribers (Inventory, Fulfillment, Email) have no idea who the publisher is. They only know about the Topic.” Message Filtering Timothy looked at the diagram. “Wait. What if someone buys a digital gift card? The Inventory and Email services need to know, but the Fulfillment service doesn’t need to print a physical shipping label. Won’t Fulfillment get clogged with digital orders?” “Excellent architectural foresight,” Margaret smiled. “We handle that with SNS Message Filtering.” She opened the subscription configuration for the fulfillment-queue. She added a JSON filter policy: “Now,” Margaret explained, “SNS inspects the incoming message from Checkout. If the JSON payload says "item_type": "digital", SNS routes copies to Inventory and Email, but it silently drops the message for Fulfillment. We don't have to write custom filtering logic in our application code; the AWS network handles it for us." Limits and Guarantees Timothy updated the Checkout code to make a single SDK call to SNS instead of multiple calls to SQS. “What if the order payload is huge?” Timothy asked. “Like a massive JSON blob containing dozens of custom items?” “SNS has a strict 256KB payload limit,” Margaret cautioned. “If your message exceeds that, we store the payload in an Amazon S3 bucket and just send the S3 URI through SNS. That is called the Claim-Check pattern.” “And remember our lesson on idempotency,” Margaret added. “Like SQS, standard SNS guarantees at-least-once delivery. Your subscribers must still be idempotent to handle the rare duplicate message. If a workflow requires strict ordering and exactly-once processing, we would use SNS FIFO topics paired with SQS FIFO queues — but for our standard Checkout flow, this is perfect.” “So if the Analytics team wants a feed of all orders next week…” Timothy started. “You don’t touch the Checkout code,” Margaret finished. “You just create an analytics-queue and subscribe it to the Topic. The architecture scales itself." Key Concepts Amazon SNS (Simple Notification Service): A fully managed Pub/Sub service for A2A (application-to-application) and A2P (application-to-person) messaging. It uses a “push” model. Publish/Subscribe (Pub/Sub): An asynchronous messaging pattern where senders (publishers) categorize published messages into classes without knowledge of which subscribers, if any, there may be. Topic: A logical access point and communication channel in SNS. Publishers send messages to topics, and subscribers receive them from topics. Fanout Pattern: An architecture where a single message published to an SNS topic is replicated and pushed to multiple Amazon SQS queues, parallelizing asynchronous processing. Message Filtering: An SNS feature that allows subscribers to specify which subset of messages they want to receive from a topic, reducing downstream compute costs. Claim-Check Pattern: A messaging pattern used to bypass size limits by storing the actual payload in a database or object store (like S3) and passing only the reference (the claim check) through the message bus. Aaron Rose is a software engineer and technology writer at tech-reader.blog   and the author of . Software engineer and technology blogger.

Please reload this page. michaelklishin on Feb 20, 2025 Maintainer Original comment in English - Translate to English @anton-ryzhov you have multiple AMQP 0-9-1 frames in frame 47, starting with a basic.deliver followed by content frames and a content body. This can happen when multiple events are "dispatched" at the same time, including a server-initiated channel.close . Queues and streams do not verify if a channel is closed in order to avoid overhead that won't detect any issues an absolute majority of the time. They expect the channel process to shut down, which takes a moment, even if that means a few milliseconds. rabbit_writer , the process that serializes all outgoing frames and writes them to the socket (for a specific connection), is not concurrent but won't verify whether all the frames are on the same channel or not, whether one of the frames is a channel.close , and so on, again, to not introduce any overhead that won't have any effect an absolute majority of the time. Simply ignore those frames in aio-pika when the channel is not open according to the library's connection state. Beta Was this translation helpful?
