Source: Architectural Taxonomy of Modern Messaging Infrastructure: Queues, Streams, and Publish-Subscribe Systems
Type: Pasted Text
Architectural Taxonomy of Modern Messaging Infrastructure: Queues, Streams, and Publish-Subscribe Systems2.3.4. Messaging InfrastructureModern distributed systems rely on asynchronous messaging infrastructure to decouple operational components, enforce architectural boundaries, and establish scalable, fault-tolerant communication pathways.[1, 2, 3] In contrast to synchronous request-response communication patterns, which introduce tight coupling, propagate runtime failures, and create latency bottlenecks, asynchronous message-oriented middleware isolates execution environments.[1, 4, 5, 6] This structural independence is achieved by shifting the responsibility of message state management, routing, and buffering from the application endpoints to dedicated brokers.[7, 8, 9] The selection of an asynchronous messaging paradigm directly shapes a system's capacity to scale horizontally, recover from transient network partitions, and maintain eventual consistency across heterogeneous databases.[2, 5, 10] To construct an optimal architecture, systems engineers must evaluate the distinct operational characteristics, mechanical pipelines, and trade-offs of the three primary asynchronous messaging styles: transient Point-to-Point Message Queues, immutable and replayable Event Streams, and highly decoupled Publish-Subscribe fabrics.[2, 10, 11]2.3.4.1. Message QueuesPoint-to-point message queues represent a classic task-distribution pattern where messages are produced and directed to a specific destination queue to be processed by a single consumer instance.[2, 7, 10] Under this paradigm, the message queue operates as a transient buffer that persists messages only until they are successfully processed and acknowledged, at which point the broker permanently deletes the data from storage.[1, 10] This lifecycle makes message queues highly suitable for background task processing, order execution pipelines, and transactional work distribution where guaranteeing once-and-only-once message processing is paramount.[1, 10, 11]Message queues historically embody the "Smart Pipes, Dumb Endpoints" design philosophy.[7] In this model, the messaging broker is highly complex, actively tracking which subscriber has received which messages, maintaining delivery states, managing message locks, and monitoring consumer load.[7] Conversely, the consumers remain merely reactive, possessing no knowledge of the overall topology and simply executing a function upon message receipt.[7]However, direct point-to-point queues introduce a coupling bottleneck often termed the "message passing" hazard.[7] Because the originating system emits an explicit message designated for a specific downstream queue, the producer must possess direct knowledge of the downstream receiver's logical endpoint.[7] If an additional service subsequently requires access to the same data stream, a new queue must be declared, and the upstream producer must be modified and redeployed to write to this new queue, violating the open-closed principle.[7]2.3.4.1.1. Point-to-Point Delivery and AMQP 0-9-1 Protocol MechanicsThe execution of point-to-point delivery relies on standardized wire protocols, with the Advanced Message Queuing Protocol (AMQP) version 0-9-1 serving as the industry benchmark for messaging middleware.[8, 12, 13] AMQP 0-9-1 is a compact, binary application-layer protocol that transmits data as structured frames.[8, 13, 14] It decouples the physical TCP connection from logical application interactions by multiplexing multiple lightweight channels over a single socket connection.[8, 15] This optimization avoids the substantial CPU and kernel-level overhead associated with continuously opening and closing TCP connections during high-throughput workloads.[15]Within the AMQP model, publishers do not write directly to queues; instead, they target logical routing agents known as exchanges.[8, 16] Exchanges evaluate incoming message properties and distribute message copies to queues based on pre-declared routing rules called bindings.[8, 13, 16]Direct exchanges route messages to queues whose binding key matches the message's routing key exactly, supporting unicast routing.[8, 13] Fanout exchanges bypass routing keys entirely, duplicating and broadcasting messages to every bound queue.[8, 13, 16] Topic exchanges facilitate complex routing by matching hierarchical routing keys against binding patterns containing wildcards, such as asterisks for single-word matching and hashes for zero or more words.[8, 16] Headers exchanges route messages based on key-value pairs declared within the message metadata header rather than routing keys.[8]The AMQP 0-9-1 protocol is highly programmable, allowing client applications to dynamically define topology and routing schemes on the broker.[16] For example, a client can declare an empty string as a queue name, instructing the broker to auto-generate a unique, random queue name.[16] Furthermore, AMQP brokers pre-declare a default exchange, which is a direct exchange implicitly bound to every queue using the queue's name as the routing key.[16, 17] This allows publishers to bypass custom exchange configurations and target queues directly.[16] Consumers interact with these queues via two patterns: a highly efficient push-based API (using basic.consume), which establishes a long-lived subscription to have messages dispatched instantly, or a highly inefficient pull-based polling API (using basic.get), which should be avoided due to the latency and CPU overhead of continuous polling.[11, 16, 18]To guarantee delivery over unreliable networks, AMQP implements a strict transactional acknowledgement protocol.[13, 18] When RabbitMQ pushes a message to a consumer, it assigns a unique, channel-scoped, monotonically increasing positive integer known as a delivery tag.[18] Because delivery tags are strictly bound to a specific channel, the consumer must execute acknowledgements on the exact channel where the message was received; attempting to acknowledge on a different channel triggers a protocol exception that terminates the connection.[18]In automatic acknowledgement mode, the broker considers a message delivered immediately after writing it to the TCP socket.[18] While this maximizes throughput by eliminating the round-trip latency of network confirmations, it is highly unsafe because messages are lost if the consumer crashes mid-process or the connection drops before the consumer reads the data from the kernel socket buffer.[18]To protect data integrity, manual acknowledgement mode keeps the message locked on the broker until the consumer returns a positive confirmation via basic.ack.[18] Consumers can batch these confirmations by setting the multiple parameter to true, which positively acknowledges all outstanding delivery tags up to and including the specified tag, reducing network traffic.[18]If processing fails, the consumer can send a negative acknowledgement via basic.reject for a single message, or basic.nack (a RabbitMQ protocol extension) to reject multiple messages simultaneously.[18] Depending on the state of the requeue flag, the broker will either return the message to the head of the queue for another consumer to process or route it to a Dead-Letter Exchange.[18]To maximize throughput in manual mode without overwhelming consumer memory, systems must tune Quality of Service (QoS) prefetch settings.[18] Prefetch limits define the maximum number of unacknowledged, in-flight messages allowed on a single channel.[13, 18] A prefetch limit of zero removes all constraints, allowing the broker to continuously push messages, which risks running the consumer out of heap memory.[18] Conversely, a conservative prefetch of one eliminates parallel processing and introduces massive network-hop delays.[18] Industry evaluations demonstrate that setting prefetch bounds between 100 and 300 yields optimal performance, after which diminishing returns occur.[18]2.3.4.1.2. Competing Consumers and Concurrency OptimizationTo scale processing throughput horizontally when queue depth increases, systems employ the Competing Consumers pattern.[5, 19, 20, 21] Under this architecture, multiple worker instances pull from a single shared queue simultaneously, and the broker ensures that each message is routed to exactly one competing worker to avoid duplicate processing.[19, 20, 21]In enterprise services such as Azure Service Bus or RabbitMQ, this is governed by locking protocols.[19, 22] The PeekLock mechanism locks a message on the queue upon receipt, rendering it invisible to other competing consumers for a configurable duration.[19, 22] If the worker completes processing successfully, it invokes an explicit message settlement to permanently delete the message.[19] If the worker crashes, the lock expires, and the message automatically reappears in the queue for other consumers to claim.[19, 22]To prevent queue bloating and maximize processing efficiency, message bodies should remain lightweight.[19] A recommended pattern involves writing the heavy data payload (e.g., medical images, large documents) to an external blob store and placing only a lightweight metadata message containing the storage URI into the queue.[19] This ensures that the broker does not experience memory saturation from large payloads.[19]Tuning concurrency on competing consumers requires careful alignment with the hardware resources of the worker instances.[22] For CPU-bound operations such as data transformation, calculation, or serialization, concurrency limits should match the physical CPU core count to prevent excessive context switching.[22] For I/O-bound operations, including database queries and HTTP API integrations, much higher concurrency limits, typically up to 32 or 64 simultaneous threads, are optimal because the threads spend significant time waiting on external network socket reads.[22] This execution model is paired with a prefetch count configured to approximately twice the concurrency limit to ensure that local worker memory remains populated with tasks without risking lock expiration.[22]If a task exceeds the lock duration, premature lock expiration can trigger duplicate processing as other workers claim the same message.[22] To mitigate this, brokers support auto-lock renewal, configuring parameters such as MaxAutoLockRenewalDuration (e.g., up to 30 minutes) to dynamically extend the lock while the worker remains active.[22]Furthermore, competing consumers break strict FIFO message ordering because different worker instances process tasks at varying speeds.[20, 21] To restore ordering constraints in a competing consumer environment, architects must implement message sessions or partition keys, which group related messages and route them to a single worker sequentially.[20, 21]If a message continuously fails due to application exceptions or structural payload errors, it is classified as a poison message.[19, 20] To prevent a poison message from infinitely looping and wasting CPU cycles, brokers track delivery attempt counts and automatically redirect the message to a Dead-Letter Queue (DLQ) when the attempt limit is exceeded, facilitating manual inspection and isolation.[19, 20]2.3.4.1.3. Queue-Based Load LevelingQueue-based load leveling acts as a buffer between upstream clients generating requests and downstream services processing them, stabilizing systems that experience highly volatile traffic spikes.[5, 23] When traffic surges, a direct synchronous call chain can exhaust database connection pools or saturate consumer CPU limits, leading to timeouts and cascaded system failures.[5, 23] By placing a message queue between the producer and consumer, the system decouples the rate of task generation from the rate of task processing.[5, 23] The queue absorbs traffic bursts immediately, permitting producers to return low-latency responses, while downstream consumers pull and process tasks at a steady, sustainable pace dictated by their local resource limits.[1, 5, 23]The immediate benefit of load leveling is structural cost optimization.[5] In a synchronous architecture, backend servers must be provisioned to handle peak load demands, leading to massive over-provisioning and wasted budget during low-traffic periods.[5] With a queue buffering traffic, backend systems can be sized for average-load steady states, significantly reducing operational expenses.[5]Additionally, the queue itself can be configured with rate-limiting policies to actively throttle the rate of requests sent to legacy databases or external third-party APIs, protecting fragile downstream systems from starvation or rate-limit penalties.[5] If the architecture requires bidirectional, synchronous-like interaction, engineers can extend this unidirectional pattern into a two-way channel using the Request-Reply pattern with correlation IDs.[5]However, this architecture introduces key trade-offs.[5, 23] First, load leveling inherently trades immediate processing guarantees for eventual consistency, introducing message transit latency as tasks queue up during bursts.[5, 23] Second, if the rate of upstream production consistently outpaces the maximum throughput of downstream consumers, the queue will grow endlessly.[5, 23] Under extreme conditions, this queue overload backlog can exhaust broker disk storage or memory, causing system degradation.[23] To prevent this, architectures must combine load leveling with monitoring, back-pressure throttling, and dynamic auto-scaling of competing consumers.[20, 23]2.3.4.2. Event StreamsEvent streams represent a shift in asynchronous architecture, moving away from broker-managed transient queues to persistent, immutable, and append-only commit logs.[10, 24, 25] While traditional message queues delete messages upon consumer confirmation, event streams treat messages as historical facts that are appended sequentially to disk and preserved indefinitely or for a defined retention window, completely independent of consumption status.[10, 24, 25] This architecture enables multiple downstream systems to read the same chronological stream of events independently, at their own pace, and replay historical events to reconstruct application state.[10, 25, 26]In contrast to the "Smart Pipes, Dumb Endpoints" model of message queues, event streaming architectures implement the "Dumb Pipes, Smart Endpoints" model.[7] The streaming broker behaves as a high-performance, append-only disk log, maintaining no state regarding which consumers have read which messages.[7, 24] Instead, the smart consumer endpoints are entirely responsible for tracking, committing, and managing their own positional offsets, which offloads computational overhead from the brokers and allows the system to scale to massive throughput levels.[7, 24, 26]2.3.4.2.1. Persistent Partitioned Logs and Operating System Performance PrimitivesThe core architectural abstraction in event streaming platforms like Apache Kafka is the topic, which is physically subdivided into one or more partitions distributed across a broker cluster.[24, 25, 26] A partition is an immutable, ordered sequence of records stored on the broker’s local filesystem.[24, 27] Every record appended to a partition is assigned a monotonically increasing, sequential identifier called an offset.[24, 25] Partitions serve as the fundamental unit of scaling and parallelism; because a single partition can only be read by one consumer in a consumer group at any given time, the partition count establishes the maximum physical concurrency of the consumption layer.[24, 25, 26]When writing records, producers can attach an optional routing key.[24, 25] Kafka hashes this key to determine the target partition, ensuring that all records with the same key are routed to the exact same partition, which guarantees strict ordering on a per-key basis.[24, 25] If no key is provided, the producer distributes records across partitions using a round-robin algorithm to balance the write load evenly.[24, 25]Physically, partitions are structured as a series of log segment files on disk.[27] The broker writes incoming events sequentially to the active segment file until it reaches a configured limit (based on segment size, typically 1\text{ GB}, or a segment time limit).[27] Once this limit is reached, the broker closes the segment and opens a new active segment.[27]To ensure fault tolerance, partitions are replicated across brokers based on a configured replication factor (typically 3).[24, 26, 27] Each partition has one Leader broker that handles all read and write requests, and one or more Follower brokers that replicate the leader's log asynchronously.[26, 27] Followers that stay within a configured replication lag window (defined by replica.lag.time.max.ms) are classified as members of the In-Sync Replicas (ISR) pool.[24]The metadata for the entire cluster—including topic configuration, partition leadership, and ISR membership—is managed by a consensus controller.[24, 26] In Kafka version 4.0 and later, this consensus is governed by KRaft (Kafka Raft) metadata mode, which completely replaces the historical reliance on Apache ZooKeeper.[24, 25, 26] KRaft runs a Raft consensus protocol across a metadata controller quorum, storing cluster metadata in an internal replicated metadata log.[24, 26] KRaft propagation reduces metadata update latency by over 10\times, allows clusters to scale beyond 1,000,000 partitions, dramatically reduces cluster startup and recovery times from minutes to seconds, and eliminates the operational complexity of managing a separate ZooKeeper cluster.[24, 26, 28]To achieve high throughput, event-streaming brokers exploit physical hardware characteristics and operating system storage primitives.[29, 30] While random disk access is highly latent due to physical drive head seek times, sequential disk write performance is remarkably fast, occasionally exceeding random memory access speeds.[29] Kafka utilizes this characteristic by immediately appending all inbound records to active segment log files sequentially, avoiding complex tree-based disk structures that require random seeking.[27, 29, 31]Rather than implementing complex, application-level caching in the JVM heap, which introduces severe garbage collection (GC) pauses at high throughput, Kafka offloads memory management to the operating system's page cache.[29, 30, 32] When producers write data, the OS caches it in unallocated system RAM.[29, 30, 32] Because the OS page cache stores data as compact, serialized bytes rather than bloated JVM objects, cache capacity is effectively doubled.[29] This unified cache remains warm even during JVM process restarts, eliminating the typical "cold start" latency spike.[29, 32] For reads, if consumers are caught up with producers, the OS serves data directly from the page cache, bypassing physical disk reads entirely.[30, 32, 33]To transfer this cached byte stream to the network socket with maximum efficiency, Kafka uses Zero-Copy data transfer.[30, 32, 34] In a traditional data copy pathway, transferring a file from disk to a network socket involves four memory copies and four CPU context switches:Disk to OS Kernel Page Cache.OS Kernel Page Cache to JVM User-Space Application Buffer.JVM User-Space Application Buffer to Socket Buffer.Socket Buffer to the Network Interface Card (NIC) Buffer.[32, 33, 34]Copying data through user space is highly inefficient when the application does not need to inspect or modify the payload.[32, 34] Under the Zero-Copy pathway, the broker utilizes Java NIO’s FileChannel.transferTo method, which compiles down to the Linux sendfile system call.[33, 34] This instructs the OS kernel to copy the data directly from the page cache to the NIC buffer, reducing the process to two memory copies and two context switches, completely bypassing user space.[32, 33, 34]In Java NIO, memory mapping (mmap) via MappedByteBuffer is selectively used for mapping small, random-access files such as partition index files (<1\text{ MB}), while sendfile via transferTo is used for large bulk log segment files (>1\text{ MB}) to achieve optimal data transfer efficiency.[34]However, this pipeline has a critical operational trade-off: enabling Transport Layer Security (TLS/SSL) encryption breaks the Zero-Copy pathway.[32] Because encryption requires the CPU to manipulate the data payload before transmission, the broker must pull the bytes into user space to encrypt them, introducing CPU overhead and context switches that degrade throughput.[32]To tune page cache efficiency in production, systems administrators must optimize system memory and resource allocation.[30] Kafka brokers should run on dedicated VMs or physical nodes so that the majority of system RAM is unallocated and available for the OS page cache.[30] Configuring system swap memory is also critical: instead of completely disabling swap, which can trigger immediate JVM termination during memory spikes, setting system swappiness to a low value (e.g., vm.swappiness=1) allows the OS to swap out completely idle background processes and libraries, maximizing active physical RAM available for the page cache.[30]In containerized environments such as Kubernetes, page cache memory is shared across all pods running on a node, creating resource competition where unrelated intensive pods can evict Kafka’s cached segments.[30] To protect page cache stability, architects must enforce node affinity and anti-affinity rules to run Kafka on isolated physical nodes.[30] Finally, when utilizing network-attached storage such as AWS EBS, administrators must monitor the burst balance to prevent disk-write throttling during log segment flushing.[30]2.3.4.2.2. Multi-Consumer Independent Offsets and Rebalance ProtocolsBecause event streams do not delete data upon delivery, multiple independent consumer groups can subscribe to the same topic simultaneously.[24, 25, 26] To track progress, each consumer group maintains its own logical offset within each partition, committing these coordinates to an internal, replicated Kafka metadata topic named __consumer_offsets.[24, 35] This separation of state means one consumer group can read at real-time speeds while a separate analytics group processes historical data at a slower pace without resource contention or mutual interference.[10, 26]When the topology of a consumer group changes—such as when a consumer instance joins or leaves, or topic partitions are modified—the broker coordinator triggers a rebalance to redistribute partition assignments.[35, 36, 37] This process incurs a significant coordination cost, historically governed by the Eager Rebalancing protocol.[21, 35, 36] Eager rebalancing enforces a "Stop-the-World" model: the moment a membership change is detected, all consumers in the group must stop fetching data, revoke ownership of all their assigned partitions, rejoin the group, and wait for a completely new global partition assignment.[21, 35, 36] During this synchronization window, consumption grinds to a halt, causing processing lag and latency spikes.[21, 35, 37]To eliminate this pause, modern Kafka clients utilize the Cooperative (Incremental) Rebalancing protocol (defined in KIP-429).[35, 36, 38] Cooperative rebalancing divides the redistribution process into two distinct phases separated by a synchronization barrier.[21, 37] In the first phase (Revocation), the group leader identifies only the specific partitions that must move to balance the load, instructing only the affected consumers to revoke those specific assignments.[21, 35, 37] Consumers holding unaffected partitions continue processing uninterrupted.[21, 35, 37] Once the synchronization barrier confirms the orphaned partitions are released, the coordinator executes the second phase (Assignment), mapping those partitions to their new owners.[21, 37]In Kafka 3.0 and later, the default partition assignment strategy configuration is set to a list containing both eager and cooperative assignors (specifically ``).[21] This allows gradual, backward-compatible migrations: the group coordinator automatically selects the first strategy supported by all active group members.[21]To prevent frequent, expensive rebalances triggered by short, transient restarts (such as rolling updates or pod rescheduling), architects can configure Static Group Membership.[21, 36, 37] This is achieved by setting a unique, stable group.instance.id for each consumer container, often derived from stable hostnames such as Kubernetes StatefulSet pod names.[21, 36, 37] When a static consumer restarts and rejoins the group within the configured session.timeout.ms window, the coordinator recognizes the stable instance ID, preserves its partition assignments, and bypasses the rebalance process entirely.[21, 36]2.3.4.2.3. High Replayability, Tiered Storage, and State ReconstructionThe persistence of the commit log provides high replayability, enabling event sourcing architectures where application state is not stored directly but reconstructed on demand by replaying the chronological log of state changes from offset zero.[1, 10, 25] Replayability simplifies debugging and auditing; if a software bug corrupts downstream database tables, engineers can patch the consumer code, reset the committed offset back in time, and reprocess the historical stream of events to rebuild a consistent state.[10, 25, 39]Preserving the entire history of an enterprise on high-performance broker disks is financially and operationally prohibitive.[24, 26, 30] To resolve this, modern architectures utilize Tiered Storage (such as KIP-405), decoupling compute from long-term storage.[24, 25, 30] Tiered storage divides partition logs into a Hot tier and a Cold tier [24, 25]:Hot Tier: Local SSD or NVMe storage on the brokers, retaining only the most recent log segments to guarantee sub-millisecond tail latencies for active consumers.[24, 30]Cold Tier: Cost-efficient remote cloud object storage, such as Amazon S3, Google Cloud Storage, or Azure Blob Storage, to retain older, closed log segments, reducing storage costs by up to 90%.[24, 25]When a consumer requests historical data (e.g., for state reconstruction), the broker seamlessly fetches the segment from cold storage, bypassing local disk constraints and reducing overall cluster storage costs.[24, 25, 30]2.3.4.3. Pub-SubThe publish-subscribe (pub-sub) model is an asynchronous messaging pattern focused on extreme structural decoupling and application-to-application (A2A) event broadcasting.[3, 4, 9, 40] In a pub-sub model, message senders (publishers) do not target specific receivers or address specific downstream queues.[3, 4, 9] Instead, publishers categorize events into logical channels called topics.[3, 4, 9] Interested downstream services register their interest by creating subscriptions to these topics.[3, 41, 42] When an event is published, the underlying message broker duplicates and fans out the event to all active subscriptions simultaneously, facilitating parallel, non-blocking execution.[1, 9, 41, 43]2.3.4.3.1. Topic-Based Broadcasting and GCP Pub/Sub SystemsIn contrast to broker-mediated pub-sub systems, which rely on central message brokers to manage routing and subscriptions, some architectural scenarios implement brokerless pub-sub designs.[4, 9] A classic example is the Data Distribution Service (DDS) middleware, which utilizes IP multicast protocols and peer-to-peer metadata discovery to establish direct, decentralized communication paths between publishers and subscribers without an intermediary broker.[4]For global enterprise scalability, managed cloud-native pub-sub systems such as Google Cloud Pub/Sub provide robust topic-based broadcasting.[44, 45, 46] Google Cloud Pub/Sub is architecturally divided into an isolated Control Plane and a Data Plane to achieve scalability, availability, and low latency.[44, 45, 46]The Control Plane manages the assignment of publishers and subscribers to physical data plane servers.[44, 45, 46, 47] Control plane servers are called routers.[44, 45, 46, 47] When a client establishes a connection, a router determines the optimal target data center based on shortest network distance (latency).[44, 45, 46, 47] Within that data center, the router distributes the load across available data plane servers using a variant of consistent hashing developed by Google Research.[44, 45, 46] Consistent hashing balances load uniformity across servers while ensuring assignment stability, minimizing the disruption of existing connections when servers fail or scale.[44, 45, 46, 47] A service proxy layer stands between the client and the data plane, abstracting connection optimization and failover logic from the application.[44, 46]The Data Plane is responsible for the actual reception, persistence, and delivery of messages.[44, 45, 46, 47] Data plane servers are called forwarders.[44, 45, 46, 47] Any individual forwarder is capable of serving clients for any topic or subscription, which allows the control plane to be upgraded without interrupting active message transmission.[44]The lifecycle of a message within the Google Cloud Pub/Sub data plane is highly structured:Publish and Encryption: A publisher client sends a message to a topic. The service proxy encrypts the payload and routes it to an assigned publishing forwarder.[44, 45]Synchronous Majority Replication: To guarantee durability, the publishing forwarder immediately writes the encrypted message to an odd number N of storage clusters.[44] The forwarder considers the write persisted only when it receives confirmation from a majority quorum of clusters, mathematically defined as: \text{Quorum} = \left\lceil \frac{N}{2} \right\rceilPublisher Acknowledgement: Once the quorum is reached, the publishing forwarder returns a persistent acknowledgement to the publisher, establishing a delivery guarantee.[44, 45]Subscribing Forwarder Delivery: Concurrently, subscribing forwarders pull unacknowledged messages from the publishing forwarder and push them to downstream subscribers (or wait for subscriber pull requests).[44, 45]Durable Deletion: The subscriber processes the message and returns an acknowledgement.[42, 45] Once an acknowledgement is received from every subscription attached to the topic, Pub/Sub asynchronously deletes the message from physical storage.[42, 45] If a subscriber fails to acknowledge within a configurable deadline, the message is redelivered.[42]Clients can interact with Pub/Sub via high-level client libraries or low-level auto-generated gRPC APIs.[42] Push subscriptions deliver messages as HTTPS POST requests to a pre-configured HTTP endpoint, whereas Pull subscriptions rely on subscribers issuing polling requests to retrieve messages.[11, 42, 47] If a message cannot be processed, subscribers can nack (negatively acknowledge) the message, setting its acknowledgement deadline (via modifyAckDeadline) to 0 to trigger immediate redelivery.[42]2.3.4.3.2. Fan-out Operations and Dynamic Endpoint IntegrationsThe primary operational advantage of pub-sub architectures is the fan-out pattern, which replicates a single event to multiple downstream receivers simultaneously.[1, 40, 41, 43, 48] A canonical enterprise pattern is the integration of AWS Simple Notification Service (SNS) with multiple AWS Simple Queue Service (SQS) queues.[40, 43, 48] SQS is a point-to-point queuing service designed for single-consumer processing.[40] While publishing directly from a service to multiple SQS queues introduces high coupling and API-call overhead, routing events through an intermediary SNS topic resolves these constraints.[40, 48] The producer publishes a single message to the SNS topic, which replicates and pushes the event into all subscribed SQS queues simultaneously.[40, 43] This ensures that downstream microservices (such as inventory, payment, and notifications) receive independent, durable copies of the event in parallel without impacting the performance of the publishing service.[1, 40]However, pub-sub broadcasting structures are subject to physical payload limits.[40] For example, Amazon SNS enforces a strict 256\text{ KB} payload limit.[40] To broadcast large payloads (such as high-definition videos or massive transactional logs) without exceeding this limit, architectures utilize the Claim-Check pattern.[3, 40] Under this pattern, the publisher writes the raw, heavy payload to an external, highly durable cloud object store (such as Amazon S3), retrieves a unique reference URI, and publishes a lightweight metadata message containing the URI through the pub-sub topic.[40] Downstream consumers receive the reference message and download the heavy payload directly from S3, bypassing broker storage limits.[40]To prevent downstream services from being overwhelmed by irrelevant events, pub-sub systems support message filtering.[40] Subscribers declare JSON-based filter policies on their subscriptions, instructing the broker to only deliver messages whose attributes match specified criteria, thereby reducing unnecessary network and compute costs.[40] If a push subscription fails to deliver messages (e.g., due to downstream endpoint timeouts or structural format errors), pub-sub brokers route the failed messages to a pre-configured dead-letter topic to avoid blocking the pipeline.[47]2.3.4.3.3. Temporal Decoupling, State Synchronization, and the Transactional Outbox PatternPub-sub systems enforce temporal decoupling, meaning that the publishing process and the subscription consumption process do not need to occur at the same time.[4, 5, 6, 10] Senders can publish events even if downstream consumer systems are offline or undergoing deployment.[4, 6, 10] The message broker buffers the events until the consumers recover and process the backlog, protecting system availability.[1, 5]However, this asynchronous, temporally decoupled model introduces a critical challenge: the transactional dual-write problem.[6] In a standard event-driven microservice, a local transaction must update a local database and publish a corresponding event to a pub-sub topic.[6] Because databases and messaging brokers operate under separate transactional scopes, these two writes lack a shared transaction boundary, introducing dangerous failure domains:If the database write commits but the subsequent network call to the messaging broker fails, the downstream system is never notified, resulting in data loss.[6]If the message is published successfully but the database transaction rolls back, downstream services process a state change that does not exist in the source database, creating system-wide inconsistency.[6]To resolve the dual-write problem and guarantee eventual consistency, architectures employ the Transactional Outbox pattern.[6, 49] Under this pattern, the application database serves as the single source of truth for both state changes and message emissions.[6] Instead of calling the messaging broker directly, the application service inserts a message record into a dedicated outbox_events database table as part of the exact same local database transaction (e.g., utilizing Spring's @Transactional annotation) that updates the business entities.[6]A separate outbox publisher process asynchronously polls the outbox table and delivers the events to the messaging broker.[6, 49] Two primary implementation strategies are used:Scheduled Poller: A simple background thread queries the outbox table at short intervals.[6] While highly customizable, frequent polling introduces database stress, and infrequent polling adds delivery latency.[6]Change Data Capture (CDC): Tools like Debezium continuously monitor the database transaction log (e.g., PostgreSQL Write-Ahead Log or MySQL binary log) to detect new outbox rows.[6] CDC captures changes with sub-millisecond latency, completely avoiding application-level polling and database query overhead.[6]The database schema of the transactional outbox table must contain specific attributes to support reliable downstream routing, ordering, and deduplication.[6]Because the outbox publisher must retry on network partitions, it operates with an at-least-once delivery guarantee, meaning duplicate messages are inevitably written to the broker.[6, 49] To achieve end-to-end exactly-once processing (EOS), downstream consumers must be designed to be completely idempotent.[6, 39, 49]This is achieved by implementing the Inbox pattern on the consumer side.[39, 49] The consumer assigns a unique eventId (often mapped to the outbox aggregate ID) to each incoming message.[6] Upon receiving a message, the consumer attempts to write the eventId to a local, dedicated inbox_events database table within the same transaction that applies the business data update.[6, 39] If a duplicate message is received, the database unique constraint on the eventId triggers a violation, allowing the consumer to reject the duplicate and commit only once, thereby simulating exactly-once processing.[6, 39, 49]To streamline the implementation of these complex consistency patterns, development teams often leverage enterprise-grade framework integration libraries such as MassTransit, NServiceBus, or CAP.[49]Architectural Comparison of Messaging ParadigmsDeveloping resilient, high-performance distributed systems requires aligning messaging infrastructure with application workload patterns.[10, 11] The quantitative selection of an asynchronous middleware engine depends on matching system requirements to the performance profiles of the candidate architectures.[11, 28, 50]Conclusions and Strategic Architectural GuidelinesAn examination of modern messaging infrastructure reveals distinct design pathways optimized for specific operational constraints and business domains.The first core conclusion is that applications requiring transactional background processing, reliable task queueing, and exact once-and-only-once execution must prioritize point-to-point Message Queues.[1, 10, 11] RabbitMQ and AWS SQS are optimized for these use cases.[11, 50] The locking mechanics, coupled with manual AMQP consumer acknowledgements and thread-concurrency settings, ensure resilient work distribution.[18, 19, 22] However, engineers must prepare for the loss of strict FIFO message ordering when scaling via competing consumers, applying message sessions or partition keys if sequential execution is required.[20, 21]The second core conclusion is that architectures requiring persistent log retention, real-time analytics ingestion, high replayability, or state reconstruction via event sourcing must deploy Event Streams such as Apache Kafka.[1, 10, 25] The performance of the append-only commit log relies on operating system primitives: bypassing the JVM heap to leverage the OS page cache, executing Zero-Copy data transfers via sendfile(), and offloading metadata to KRaft controller quorums.[24, 29, 32, 34] To minimize the rebalancing latency tax during container rolling updates, static group membership must be implemented.[21, 36]The third core conclusion is that enterprise microservices requiring temporal and physical decoupling, high network scalability, and one-to-many broadcasting must implement Publish-Subscribe systems.[4, 9, 40] Systems like AWS SNS or GCP Pub/Sub replicate and fan out events across independent subscriptions.[40, 43, 44] To resolve the dual-write consistency hazard, teams must integrate the Transactional Outbox pattern on the producing service, using CDC or polling, and enforce strict consumer-side idempotency via the Inbox pattern to handle duplicate deliveries gracefully.[6, 39, 49]

Dismiss Got it On this page Judging Performance of a Messaging Service Scalability Availability Latency Pub/Sub Basic Architecture Control Plane Data Plane - The Life of a Message Keeping Pub/Sub Up and Running Environments Rollout Monitoring Controls Summary Glossary Pub/Sub is an asynchronous messaging service designed to be highly reliable and scalable. The service is built on a core Google infrastructure component that many Google products have relied upon for over a decade. Google products including Ads, Search and Gmail use this infrastructure to send over 500 million messages per second, totaling over 1TB/s of data. This article describes the salient design features that enables Pub/Sub to provide this type of scale reliably. Judging Performance of a Messaging Service A messaging service like Pub/Sub can be judged on its performance in three aspects: scalability, availability, and latency. These three factors are often at odds with each other, requiring compromises on one to improve the other two. The terms "scalability," “availability,” and “latency” can refer to different properties of a system, so the following sections describe how they are defined in Pub/Sub. Scalability A scalable service should be able to handle increases in load without noticeable degradation of latency or availability. "Load" can refer to various dimensions of usage in Pub/Sub: Number of topics Number of publishers Number of subscriptions Number of subscribers Number of messages Size of messages Rate of messages (throughput) published or consumed Size of backlog on any given subscription Availability In a distributed system, the types and severity of problems can vary greatly. A system's availability is measured on how well it deals with different types of issues, gracefully failing over in a way that is unnoticeable to end users. Failures can occur in hardware (e.g., disk drives not working or network connectivity problems), in software, and due to load. Failure due to load could happen when a sudden increase in traffic in the service (or in other software components running on the same hardware or in software dependencies) results in resource scarcity. Availability can also degrade due to human error, where one makes mistakes in building or deploying software or configurations. Latency Latency is a time-based measure of the performance of a system. A service generally wants to minimize latency wherever possible. For Pub/Sub, the two most important latency metrics are: The amount of time it takes to acknowledge a published message. The amount of time it takes to deliver a published message to a subscriber. Pub/ Sub Basic Architecture This section explains the design of Pub/Sub to show how the service attains its scalability and low latency while retaining availability. The system is designed to be horizontally scalable, where an increase in the number of topics, subscriptions, or messages can be handled by increasing the number of instances of running servers. Pub/Sub servers run in all Google Cloud regions around the world. This allows the service to offer fast, global data access, while giving users control over where messages are stored. Cloud Pub/Sub offers global data access in that publisher and subscriber clients are not aware of the location of the servers to which they connect or how those services route the data. Pub/Sub's load balancing mechanisms direct publisher traffic to the nearest Google Cloud data center where data storage is allowed, as defined in the Resource Location Restriction section of the IAM & admin console. This means that publishers in multiple regions may publish messages to a single topic with low latency. Any individual message is stored in a single region. However, a topic may have messages stored in many regions. When a subscriber client requests messages published to this topic, it connects to the nearest server which aggregates data from all messages published to the topic for delivery to the client. Pub/Sub is divided into two primary parts: the data plane, which handles moving messages between publishers and subscribers, and the control plane, which handles the assignment of publishers and subscribers to servers on the data plane. The servers in the data plane are called forwarders, and the servers in the control plane are called routers. When publishers and subscribers are connected to their assigned forwarders, they do not need any information from the routers (as long as those forwarders remain accessible). Therefore, it is possible to upgrade the control plane of Pub/Sub without affecting any clients that are already connected and sending or receiving messages. Control Plane The Pub/Sub control plane distributes clients to forwarders in a way that provides scalability, availability, and low latency for all clients. Any forwarder is capable of serving clients for any topic or subscription. When a client connects to Pub/Sub, the router decides the data centers the client should connect to based on shortest network distance, a measure of the latency on the connection between two points. Within any given data center the router tries to distribute overall load across the set of available forwarders. The router must balance two different goals when performing this assignment: (a) uniformity of load (i.e., ideally every forwarder is equally loaded); and (b) stability of assignments (i.e., ideally a change in load or a change in the set of available forwarders changes the smallest number of existing assignments). The router uses a variant of consistent hashing developed by Google Research to achieve a tunable balance between consistency and uniformity. The router provides the client with an ordered list of forwarders it can consider connecting to. This ordered list may change based on forwarder availability and the shape of the load from the client. A client takes this list of forwarders and connects to one or more of them. The client prefers connecting to the forwarders most recommended by the router, but also takes into consideration any failures that have occurred, e.g., it may decide to try forwarders in a different data center if several attempts to the nearest ones have failed. In order to abstract Pub/Sub clients away from these implementation details, there is a service proxy between the clients and forwarders that performs this connection optimization on behalf of clients. Data Plane - The Life of a Message The data plane receives messages from publishers and sends them to clients. Perhaps the best way of understanding Pub/Sub's data plane is by looking at the life of a message, from the moment it is received by the service to the moment it is no longer present in the service. Let us trace the steps of processing a message. For the purposes of this section, we assume that the topic on which the message is published has at least one subscription attached to it. In general, a message goes through these steps: A publisher sends a message. The message is written to storage. Pub/Sub sends an acknowledgment to the publisher that it has received the message and guarantees its delivery to all attached subscriptions. At the same time as writing the message to storage, Pub/Sub delivers it to subscribers. Subscribers send an acknowledgment to Pub/Sub that they have processed the message. Once at least one subscriber for each subscription has acknowledged the message, Pub/Sub deletes the message from storage. First, a publisher sends a message on a topic to Pub/Sub. It is encrypted by the proxy layer and sent to a publishing forwarder, a forwarder to which the publisher is connected. In order to ensure delivery, the message is immediately written to storage. The forwarder initially writes the message to N clusters (where N is an odd number) and considers the message persisted when it has been written to at least ⌈N/2⌉ clusters. Once a message is persisted, the publishing forwarder acknowledges receipt of the message back to the publisher, at which point Pub/Sub guarantees that the message will be delivered to all attached subscriptions. Within each cluster, the message is written to M independent disks (where M is an odd number), requiring the data to be on ⌈M/2⌉ disks before it is considered persisted in that cluster. In total, any message published will be written to at least ⌈M/2⌉ independent disks in ⌈N/2⌉ clusters before it is considered persisted and will eventually be replicated to NM disks.The publishing forwarder has a list of all subscriptions that are attached to a topic. It is responsible for persisting both the published messages and the metadata describing which messages have been acknowledged by each subscription. The set of messages received and stored by a publishing forwarder for a particular topic, along with this tracking of acknowledged messages, is called a "publish message source." Depending on the throughput requirements for the topic, a single publisher may send its messages to multiple publishing forwarders and store messages in multiple publish message sources. Different publishers for the same topic may also send messages to different publishing forwarders. Each message is sent to only a single publishing forwarder. Pub/Sub dynamically tunes the number of publishing forwarders that receive messages for a particular topic as the throughput changes.Subscribers receive messages by connecting to subscribing forwarders, forwarders through which messages flow to subscribers from publishers. “Connecting” in the case of a pull subscriber means issuing a pull request. “Connecting” in the case of a push subscriber means having the push endpoint registered with Pub/Sub. Once a subscription is created, it is guaranteed that any messages published after that point will be delivered to that subscription, what we call a sync-point guarantee.Each subscribing forwarder needs to request messages from publishing forwarders that have publish message sources for the topic. Like publishers, subscribers may connect to more than one subscribing forwarder in order to receive messages. That way, not every subscribing forwarder needs to be aware of or receive messages from every publish message source for a topic--an important property for Pub/Sub to be able to scale horizontally. Based on the throughput of messages being delivered to subscribers, Pub/Sub dynamically tunes the number of subscribing forwarders through which subscribers receive messages for a particular topic as the throughput changes.A subscribing forwarder makes requests to one or more publishing forwarders that have publish message sources for a topic to ask for the messages it needs. The publishing forwarder sends the unacknowledged messages to the subscribing forwarder, which then relays the messages to a subscriber.Once a subscriber processes a message, it sends an acknowledgment back to the subscribing forwarder. The subscribing forwarder relays this acknowledgment to the publishing forwarder, which stores the acknowledgment in the publish message source. Once all subscriptions on a topic have acknowledged a message, the message is asynchronously deleted from the publish message source and from storage.Different messages for a single topic and subscription can flow through many publishers, subscribers, publishing forwarders, and subscribing forwarders. Publishers can publish to multiple forwarders simultaneously and subscribers may connect to multiple subscribing forwarders to receive messages. Therefore, the flow of messages through connections among publishers, subscribers, and forwarders can be complex. The following diagram shows how messages could flow for a single topic and subscription, where different colors indicate the different paths messages may take from publishers to subscribers:Keeping Pub/ Sub Up and RunningEnsuring that a distributed system like Pub/Sub can stay up and running and effectively serve all customers requires a great deal of visibility into and control of the system. Maintaining the service is the responsibility of our Site Reliability Engineers (SREs). For Pub/Sub, these engineers are based in multiple locations around the world in order to provide 24/7 coverage.EnvironmentsThe first part of maintaining a system like Pub/Sub is to have the ability to test software before it is used by customers. In order to make that possible, there are three Pub/Sub environments: test, staging, and production. Test and staging do not contain any customer traffic; they contain only our continuously-running tests and monitoring that help to find any issues with releases. These environments receive new releases of the software before production. The difference between test and staging is that the latter is an exact replica of what is in (or will very shortly be in) the production environment, including software version and command-line flags. The former may have features enabled that developers are currently working on and plan for release sometime in the future.RolloutThe procedure for rolling out and testing Pub/Sub is designed to minimize potential impact. Let's look at the typical steps for the rollout of a new version of Pub/Sub:Ensure all unit tests and integration tests pass.Build a new version of all the servers.Deploy the new servers to the test and staging environments.Run the servers on the test and staging environment for several days.If there are no known issues, release servers to canary, a subset of the production environment that has a small amount of customer traffic.If no problems are detected in canary, progressively roll the servers out to more of production over several days until they are released everywhere.Since Pub/Sub is designed to be resilient to failures, e.g., through the separation of the control plane and data plane, rollouts of new versions of servers are seamless to customers and should have no impact on the performance they see.MonitoringThe key to keeping Pub/Sub up and running is to automatically detect and mitigate issues before they become visible to end users. Accomplishing this requires extensive monitoring of the system. The Site Reliability Engineering team (SREs) maintain a set of service level indicators ( SLIs), well-defined metrics that describe the behavior of the system. Metrics may include "amount of time it takes for a CreateSubscription request to complete" or "rate of errors generated by Publish requests." These metrics are measured in a variety of ways. Some of them are strictly internal to our forwarders and routers. For example, they measure how long it takes to write messages to disk.All of these measures help to define internal service level objectives ( SLOs), specific targets for the SLIs. For example, "a CreateSubscription request should take no more than five seconds to complete." SREs are alerted for SLO violations, and must attend to alerts within five minutes.A service level agreement ( SLA)* lists the SLOs that define our performance guarantees to our end users and the consequences if we don't meet them. You can read Pub/Sub's SLA. We maintain a set of clients that predictably publish and subscribe. These are called probers. Probers exist for both the data plane and the control plane. Each of our probers performs specific actions just as a customer would and measures how long the operations take. For example, we have a prober that creates a new subscription, publishes a message, and sees how long it took to both create the subscription and receive the message. If the probers determine that any of the measured metrics are not what is expected, SREs are alerted. The metrics for our servers and probers are summarized on several internal dashboards, the first place SREs look whenever diagnosing potential issues. These pages provide quick access to stats and graphs of the entire service. They can also be broken down by topic, data center, or individual task. The metrics that are most interesting to users of the service are exposed through Google Cloud Monitoring. Controls We have at our disposal several controls to help tune the performance of Pub/Sub. Some of these controls are designed to help with data center or machine outages. We can place routing constraints on some or all topics, which are rules specifying sets of clients that can and/or cannot connect to sets of forwarders. We use routing constraints to drain traffic away from individual forwarder tasks or whole data centers that are not functioning as expected. Another tunable feature we have is flow control. This feature allows us to maximize throughput while preventing overload in the service. Flow control is a form of traffic shaping whereby sudden unexpected spikes in load can be smoothed out over time for greater service stability. Flow control operates system-wide or on a per-topic or per-subscriber basis to limit the number of messages or the number of bytes that are transferred or are outstanding. In this case "outstanding" means delivered to the client, but not yet acknowledged. Both flow control and routing constraints allow us to optimize the Pub/Sub's performance without customers having to worry about these low-level details. Summary The advantages in scalability, availability, and latency of a service like Pub/Sub define the value proposition for customers who are considering a move to managed cloud services. Any asynchronous messaging service has to be built from the ground up with these features in mind. With over a decade of experience in reliably delivering lots of messages quickly, the Pub/Sub team has built and maintains a service that can keep up with the demands of the most fundamental products at Google. Now that same service is available to all external customers who want to send their messages around the world without having to worry whether or not their messaging system can handle 2x, 10x, or 100x their current load. Glossary Was this helpful? Source: Connections - RabbitMQ
URL: https://www.rabbitmq.com/docs/connections
Connections | RabbitMQ Skip to main content 🎉 RabbitMQ 4.3 is out! 🥳 Getting Started Docs Blog Support Commercial Features 4.3 Release series Next 4.3 4.2 4.1 4.0 3.13 GitHub Search Ctrl K Getting Started Docs Blog Versions Release series Next 4.3 4.2 4.1 4.0 3.13 Support Commercial Features GitHub ← Back to main menu Introduction Release Information Getting Started Install and Upgrade How to Use RabbitMQ Publishing Messages Exchanges Consuming Messages Queues Streams Channels Reliability and Data Safety Consumer Acknowledgements and Publisher Confirms Network Distribution Plugins Protocols AMQP 1.0 Connections Inter-Protocol Property Conversion Heartbeats AMQP 0-9-1 Extensions Intercepting Messages Client Libraries How to Manage RabbitMQ How to Monitor RabbitMQ How to Use RabbitMQ Protocols Connections Version: 4.3 On this page Connections Overview This guide covers various topics related to connections except for network tuning or most networking-related topics. Those are covered by the Networking and Troubleshooting Networking guides. RabbitMQ supports several protocols: AMQP 0-9-1 with extensions AMQP 1.0 RabbitMQ Stream Protocol MQTT 3.1, 3.1.1, and 5.0 STOMP 1.0 through 1.2 Many topics in this guide are equally applicable to all protocols. When that's not the case, the guide tries to highlight protocol-specific features and practices. Channels is a closely related concept in AMQP 0-9-1 which is also covered in a separate guide. This guide covers: The basics of how clients use RabbitMQ Connection lifecycle How to encrypt traffic on client connections How clients can provide a connection name for easier troubleshooting Connection event logging Monitoring of connections and how to detect high connection churn scenarios Sustaining a large number of concurrent connections TLS Flow control Connection exceptions (protocol errors) Client properties and capabilities Network failure recovery and other topics related to connections. The Basics Applications interact with RabbitMQ using client libraries. There are client libraries available for many programming languages and platforms. Each protocol has its own set of client libraries. Most client libraries are open source. Both client libraries and applications that use them are referred to as "clients" in this guide. Where the difference matters, a more specific term is used (e.g. "application"). All protocols supported by RabbitMQ are TCP-based and assume long-lived connections (a new connection is not opened per protocol operation) for efficiency. One client library connection uses a single TCP connection. In order for a client to successfully connect, target RabbitMQ node must allow for connections on a certain protocol-specific port. After a client connects and successfully authenticates with a RabbitMQ node, it can publish and consume messages, define topology and perform other operations that are provided in the protocol and supported both by the client library and the target RabbitMQ node. Since connections are meant to be long-lived, clients usually consume messages by registering a subscription and having messages delivered (pushed) to them instead of polling. Clients that cannot keep a long-lived connection can use a special proxy to help reduce connection churn. When a connection is no longer necessary, applications must close them to conserve resources. Apps that fail to do it run the risk of eventually exhausting its target node of resources. Operating systems have a limit around how many TCP connections (sockets) a single process can have open simultaneously. The limit is often sufficient for development and some QA environments. Production environments must be configured to use a higher limit in order to support a larger number of concurrent client connections. Protocol Differences Different messaging protocols use different ports. Ports also vary for plain TCP and TLS-enabled connections. The Networking guide covers all ports used by RabbitMQ depending on what protocols are enabled, whether TLS is used and so on. AMQP 0-9-1 AMQP 0-9-1 provides a way for connections to multiplex over a single TCP connection. That means an application can open multiple "lightweight connections" called channels on a single connection. AMQP 0-9-1 clients open one or more channels after connecting and perform protocol operations (manage topology, publish, consume) on the channels. AMQP 1.0 AMQP 1.0 provides a way for connections to multiplex over a single TCP connection. That means an application can open multiple "lightweight connections" called sessions on a single connection. Applications then set up one or more links to publish and consume messages. Connection Lifecycle In order for a client to interact with RabbitMQ it must first open a connection. This process involves a number of steps: Application configures the client library it uses to use a certain connection endpoint (e.g. hostname and port) The library resolves the hostname to one or more IP addresses The library opens a TCP connection to the target IP address and port After the server has accepted the TCP connection, protocol-specific negotiation procedure is performed The server then authenticates the client The client now can perform operations, each of which involves an authorisation check by the server. The client retains the connections for as long as it needs to communicate with RabbitMQ This flow doesn't change significantly from protocol to protocol but there are minor differences. Protocol Differences AMQP 0-9-1 AMQP 0-9-1 has a model that includes connections and channels. Channels allow for connection multiplexing (having multiple logical connections on a "physical" or TCP one). The maximum number of channels that can be open on a connection simultaneously is negotiated by client and server at connection time. The client cannot be configured to allow for more channels than the server configured maximum. After successfully opening a connection and authenticating, applications open one or more channels and use them to perform protocol operations, e.g. define topology, consume and publish messages. AMQP 0-9-1 supports different authentication mechanisms. While it's most common for applications to provide a pair of credentials, x509 certificates and PKI can be used instead. AMQP 1.0 AMQP 1.0 has a model that includes connections, sessions and links. After successfully opening a connection and authenticating, an application opens one or more sessions. It then attaches links to the session in order to publish and consume messages. MQTT MQTT connections follow the flow described above. MQTT supports optional authentication. When it is used, RabbitMQ uses a pre-configured set of credentials. STOMP STOMP connections follow the flow described above. Connection Traffic Encryption with TLS All protocols supported by RabbitMQ allow for "clear text" (unencrypted) traffic, in other words, TLS is not a requirement. However, using TLS is highly recommended for production systems to prevent both traffic snopping and man-in-the-middle attacks. Applications that use unencrypted connections will also send credentials as "clear text". Certain security scanners will report this as "AMQP Cleartext Authentication". The solution is to use TLS for those client connections. To learn more, please refer to the guides dedicated to TLS: TLS for client connections, securing intra-cluster communication with TLS and troubleshooting TLS. Logging RabbitMQ logs all inbound client connections that send at least 1 byte of data. Connections that are opened without any activity will not be logged. This is to prevent TCP load balancer health checks from flooding the logs. Successful authentication, clean and unexpected connection closure will also be logged. This topic is covered in more detail in the Logging guide. Monitoring Number of currently open client connections and connection opening/closure rates are important metrics of the system that should be monitored. Monitoring them will help detect a number of problems that are common in messaging-based system: Connection leaks High connection churn Both problems eventually lead to node exhaustion of resources. Connection Leaks A connection leak is a condition under which an application repeatedly opens connections without closing them, or at least closing only some of them. Connection leaks eventually exhaust the node (or multiple target nodes) of file handles, which means any new inbound client, peer or CLI tool connection will be rejected. A build-up in the number of concurrent connections also increases node's memory consumption. Relevant Metrics Management UI provides a chart of the total number of connections opened cluster-wide: A connection leak on monitoring charts can be identified as a monotonically growing number of client connections. It is also possible to see how many file handles and sockets does a specific node have, which can be useful in determining connection leaks as well. The following chart demonstrates a very stable number of sockets open on a node: This chart demonstrates a monotonically growing number of connections after a drop: If the number of sockets used by a node keeps growing and growing, it is likely an indication of a connection leak in one of the applications. Some client libraries, such as the Java client, expose metrics including the number of currently opened connections. Charting and monitoring application metrics around connections is the best way to identify what app leaks connections or uses them in a suboptimal way. In many applications that use long-lived connections and do not leak them the number of connections grows on application start and then moderates (stays mostly stable with little fluctuation). Management UI provides a chart on the rate of newly opened connections as of RabbitMQ 3.7.9. Below is a chart that demonstrates a fairly low new connection rate: High Connection Churn A system is said to have high connection churn when its rate of newly opened connections is consistently high and its rate of closed connection is consistently high. This usually means that an application uses short lived connections. While with some workloads this scenario is difficult to avoid, long lived connections should be used instead when possible. RabbitMQ collects metrics on connection churn and exposes them via Prometheus and Grafana as well as management UI churn rate chart. Below is a chart that demonstrates a fairly low connection churn with a comparable number of connections open and closed in the given period of time: While connection and disconnection rates are system-specific, rates consistently above 100/second likely indicate a suboptimal connection management approach by one or more applications and usually are worth investigating. Some clients and runtimes (notably PHP) do not use long-lived connections and high connection churn rates are expected from them. A specialized proxy should be used with those clients to mitigate the churn they naturally create. Environments that experience high connection churn require TCP stack tuning to avoid resource exhaustion under churn. Resource Usage Every connection consumes memory and one file handle on the target RabbitMQ node. Most of the memory is used by connection's TCP buffers. Their size can be reduced significantly, which leads to significant per-connection memory consumption savings at the cost of a comparable reduction in connection throughput. The maximum number of file handles a RabbitMQ node can have open is limited by the kernel and must be raised in order to support a large number of connections. Supporting a Large Number of Connections In some environments it's natural to have a large number of concurrently connected clients. For example, systems that involve a large number of hardware clients (the Internet of Things a.k.a. IoT workloads) can have many thousands of clients from day one. Since connections consume resources, sustaining a large number of concurrent connections requires reducing resource consumption or provisioning more resources or nodes. In practice those two options are used in combination. A large number of concurrent connections will generate a lot of metric (stats) emission events. This increases CPU consumption even with mostly idle connections. To reduce this footprint, increase the statistics collection interval using the collect_statistics_interval key: The default is 5 seconds (5000 milliseconds). Increasing the interval value to 30-60s will reduce CPU footprint and peak memory consumption. This comes with a downside: with the value in the example above, metrics of said entities will refresh every 60 seconds. This can be perfectly reasonable in an externally monitored production system but will make management UI less convenient to use for operators. The Networking guide has a section dedicated to tuning for a large number of concurrent connections. It explains how to reduce per-connection memory footprint. Flow Control Connections that publish messages can outpace other parts of the system, most likely busy queues and queues that perform replication. When that happens, flow control is applied to publishing connections. Connections that only consume messages are not affected by the flow control applied to publishers. It is therefore recommended that, when possible, publishers and consumers use separate connections so that consumers are isolated from potential flow control that may be applied to publishing connections, affecting manual consumer acknowledgements. With slower consumers that use automatic acknowledgement mode it is very likely that connections and channels will experience flow control when writing to the TCP socket. Monitoring systems can collect metrics on the number of connections in flow state. Applications that experience flow control regularly may consider to use separate connections to publish and consume to avoid flow control effects on non-publishing operations (e.g. queue management). Error Handling and Protocol Exceptions A connection can fail or be unable to satisfy a client operation. Such scenarios are called errors or protocol exceptions. They can indicate a transient condition (e.g. a resource is locked), a semantic issue, or a protocol implementation (e.g. incorrect framing). Most errors in messaging protocols are considered to be unrecoverable. Note that protocol errors are different from network connectivity failures. Protocol Differences AMQP 0-9-1 In AMQP 0-9-1, connection errors are used to communicate unrecoverable ("hard") errors, such as incorrect framing or connection state violations. For example, if a channel with the same ID (number) is opened more than once. After sending an error to the client, the connection is closed. Errors that can be corrected and retried are communicated using channel exceptions ("soft errors"). AMQP 1.0 In AMQP 1.0, most errors fall into either session errors or link errors. A session error is unrecoverable and leads to all operations received by the peer that's detected the error to be discarded until session termination. A link error is limited to a particular link. Since links can be attached and reattached without affecting their session, in practice applications can retry an operation that's failed after correcting the root cause (if possible). STOMP In STOMP, the server communicates errors by sending an ERROR frame and closing TCP connection. The frame will contain an error message in the message field. MQTT 3.1 In MQTT 3.1, servers have a limited number of ways to communicate an error to a client. The primary way is to close client's TCP connection. This provides little context and limited visibility for developers. This is a fundamental limitation of MQTT 3.1 design. Quite often MQTT clients are configured to automatically reconnect and retry operations, potentially creating loops of error triggering, connection storms and variations of the thundering herd problem. With MQTT, inspecting server logs and monitoring connections is therefore of particular importance. Client-Provided Connection Name RabbitMQ nodes have a limited amount of information about their clients: their TCP endpoint (source IP address and port) the credentials used This information alone can make identifying applications and instances problematic, in particular when credentials can be shared and clients connect over a load balancer but Proxy protocol cannot be enabled. To make it easier to identify clients in server logs and management UI, AMQP 0-9-1 client connections, including the RabbitMQ Java client, can provide a custom identifier. If set, the identifier will be mentioned in log entries and management UI. The identifier is known as the client-provided connection name. The name can be used to identify an application or a specific component within an application. The name is optional; however, developers are strongly encouraged to provide one as it would significantly simplify certain operational tasks. Connection name must be specified using the "connection_name" field in the client capabilities table. Some client libraries, e.g. Java and .NET, provide more convenient ways of setting a custom name on a connection. Client and Server Capabilities Some protocols, namely AMQP 0-9-1, provide for clients and servers to express their capabilities when opening a connection. This can be thought of as a table of optional features that specific versions of RabbitMQ and client libraries may or may not support. This mechanism is similar to feature flags used by RabbitMQ nodes to determine what set of features is supported by all cluster members, and if a new member would be able to join the cluster. The values for these capability keys are typically booleans, indicating whether or not the capability is supported, but may vary based on the nature of the capability. For example, capability table presented by a RabbitMQ node to a client may look like (the format presented here can be thought of as pseudocode, since the actual table encoding is in a binary format and would not be human-friendly): The capabilities table for clients is optional: failure to present such a table does not preclude the client from being able to use extensions such as exchange to exchange bindings. However, in some cases such as consumer cancellation notification, the client must present the associated capability, otherwise RabbitMQ nodes will have no way of knowing that the client is capable of receiving the additional notifications. Recovery from Network Connection Failures Client's TCP connection can fail or experience serious packet loss that would make RabbitMQ nodes consider them unavailable. Some client libraries provide a mechanism for automatic recovery from network connection failures. RabbitMQ Java client and RabbitMQ .NET client support such feature, for example. This feature is largely protocol- and client library-specific. Other clients may consider network failure recovery to be a responsibility of the application. In this case they usually provide examples that include connection and topology recovery.
